<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="introduction-to-virology">Introduction to Virology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction-to-virology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="learning-objectives">Learning Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#learning-objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>At the end of the Lesson, you will be able to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Describe the history of virology &amp; explain how the present state of our knowledge of viruses was achieved</li>
<li>Define what a virus is, and explain how viruses differ from other organisms</li>
<li>Describe the essential properties of viruses</li>
<li>Explain the structure/morphology of virus and its compositions</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="introduction-to-virology">Introduction to Virology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction-to-virology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Virology is a field within microbiology that encompasses the study of viruses. Medical virology deals with the study of medically important viruses and the diseases they cause or their effect on human beings. Viruses are particles consisting of protein and nucleic acid (an RNA or DNA genome). Lack both cellular structure and independent metabolic processes. They replicate solely by exploiting living cells based on the information in the viral genome.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-history-of-virology">The History of Virology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-history-of-virology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The first written record of a virus infection is from ancient Egypt (3700BC), which shows a temple priest with typical signs of paralytic poliomyelitis. Pharaoh Ramses V, who died in 1196BC and whose well-preserved mummified body is now in a Cairo museum, is believed to have died from smallpox. The comparison between the pustular lesions on the face of this mummy and those of more recent patients is startling, and traces of smallpox epidemic in the family of Ramses V of the Egyptian 20th dynasty proved by electron microscopy and immunology.</p>
<p>Smallpox was endemic in China by 1000BC. In response, the practice of variolation was developed. Recognizing that survivors of smallpox outbreaks were protected from subsequent infection, people inhaled the dried crusts from smallpox lesions like snuff or, in later modifications, inoculated the pus from a lesion into a scratch on the forearm. Variolation was practiced for centuries and was shown to be an effective method of disease prevention, although risky because the outcome of the inoculation was never certain.</p>
<p>Edward Jenner was nearly killed by variolation at the age of seven! Not surprisingly, this experience encouraged him on to find a safer alternative treatment. On May 14, 1796, he used cowpox-infected material obtained from the hand of Sarah Nemes, a milkmaid from his home village in England, to successfully vaccinate 8-year-old James Phipps. Although initially controversial, vaccination against smallpox was almost universally adopted worldwide during the 19th Century.</p>
<p>This early success (vaccine protection) was an achievement of scientific observation and reasoning was not based on any real understanding of the nature of infectious agents. This arose separately from another line of reasoning. Antony van Leeuwenhoek (1632-1723), a Dutch merchant, constructed the first simple microscopes and with these identified bacteria as the "animalcules" he saw in his specimens. However, it was not until Robert Koch and</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020
By Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Louis Pasteur in the 1880s jointly proposed the "germ theory" of disease that the significance of these organisms became apparent.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="germ-theory-of-disease-koch-s-postulates">Germ theory of disease - Koch's postulates<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#germ-theory-of-disease-koch-s-postulates" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Koch defined four famous criteria, which are now known as Koch's postulates and still generally regarded as the proof that an infectious agent is responsible for a specific disease:</p>
<ul>
<li>The agent must be present in every case of the disease.</li>
<li>The agent must be isolated from the host and grown in vitro.</li>
<li>The disease must be reproduced when a pure culture of the agent is inoculated into a healthy susceptible host.</li>
<li>The same agent must be recovered once again from the experimentally infected host.</li>
</ul>
<p>Subsequently, Pasteur worked extensively on rabies, which he identified as being caused by a virus (from the Latin for "poison"), but despite this he did not discriminate between bacteria and other agents of disease.</p>
<ul>
<li>In 1892, Dimitri Iwanowski, a Russian botanist, showed that extracts from diseased tobacco plants could transmit disease to other plants after being passed through ceramic filters fine enough to retain the smallest known bacteria. Unfortunately, he did not realize the full significance of these results.</li>
<li>A few years later (1898), Martinus Beijerinck confirmed and extended Iwanowski's results on tobaccomosaic virus (TMV) and was the first to develop the modern idea of the virus, which he referred to as contagium vivum fluidum (soluble living germ).</li>
<li>Freidrich Loeffler and Paul Frosch (1898) showed that a similar agent was responsible for foot-and-mouth disease in cattle</li>
</ul>
<p>But, despite the realization that these new found agents caused disease in animals as well as plants, people would not accept the idea that they might have anything to do with human diseases. This resistance was finally dispelled in 1909 by Karl Landsteiner and Erwin Popper, who showed that poliomyelitis was caused by a "filterable agent" - the first human disease to be recognized as being caused by a virus.</p>
<ul>
<li>Frederick Twort (1915) and Felix d'Herelle (1917) were the first to recognize viruses that infect bacteria, which d'Herelle called bacteriophages (eaters of bacteria)</li>
<li>In the 1930s and subsequent decades, pioneering virologists such as Salvador Luria, Max Delbruck, and others used these viruses as model systems to investigate many aspects of virology, including virus structure, genetics, and replication</li>
</ul>
<p>These relatively simple agents have since proved to be very important to our understanding of all types of viruses, including those of humans, which can be much more difficult to propagate and study. The further history of virology is the story of the development of experimental tools and systems with which viruses could be examined and that opened up whole new areas of biology, including not only the biology of the viruses themselves but inevitably also the biology of the host cells on which they are dependent.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10), May 2020
This document is available on
by Belay T.

<p>☐ Virus diversity</p>
<p>There is more biological diversity within viruses than in all the rest of the bacterial, plant &amp; animal kingdoms put together. This results from the success of viruses in parasitizing all known groups of living organisms. Understanding this diversity is the key to comprehending the interactions of viruses with their hosts. At a molecular level, protein-protein, protein-nucleic acid, &amp; protein-lipid interactions determine the structure of virus particles, the synthesis &amp; expression of virus genomes &amp; the effects of viruses on the host cell.</p>
<p>☐ Viruses are distinct from living organisms</p>
<p>Viruses are submicroscopic, obligate intracellular parasites; they can only be seen with a special, very powerful microscope called an "electron microscope." However, a few groups of prokaryotic organisms that have specialized intracellular parasitic life-cycles &amp; which puzzle the above definition. The Rickettsiae &amp; Chlamydiae - obligate intracellular parasitic bacteria which have evolved so that they can exist outside the cells of their hosts only for a short period of time before losing viability. Therefore, it is necessary to add further clauses to the definition of what constitutes a virus.</p>
<p>Viruses differ from other microorganisms in a number of characteristics:</p>
<ul>
<li>they have no cellular structure, consisting only of proteins and nucleic acid (DNA or RNA)</li>
<li>They have no metabolic systems of their own, but rather depend on the synthetic mechanism of a living host cell. Viruses exploit normal cellular metabolism by delivering their own genetic information, i.e., nucleic acid, into the host cell. One thus might call viruses “vagabond genes”</li>
<li>Viruses infect other organism: bacteria (so-called bacteriophages), plants, animals, and humans.</li>
</ul>
<p>☐ Virus definition</p>
<p>Viruses are particles produced from the assembly of pre-formed components, whereas other agents grow from an increase in the integrated sum of their components &amp; reproduce by division. Virus particles (virions) themselves do not grow or undergo division. Viruses lack the genetic information which encodes apparatus necessary for the generation of metabolic energy or for protein synthesis (ribosomes)</p>
<p>☐ Viruses are energy parasites</p>
<p>No known virus has the biochemical or genetic potential to generate the energy necessary to drive all biological processes (e.g. macromolecular synthesis). They are therefore absolutely dependent on the host cell for this function.</p>
<p>Are viruses are alive?</p>
<ul>
<li>One view is that inside the host cell, viruses are alive, whereas outside it they are merely complex assemblages of metabolically inert chemicals. Chemical changes may occur in extracellular virus particles, but these are in no sense the 'growth' of a living organism.</li>
<li>Viruses are infectious agents with both living and nonliving characteristics.</li>
</ul>
<p>Living characteristics of viruses:</p>
<ul>
<li>They reproduce at a fantastic rate, but only in living host cells; they can mutate</li>
</ul>

By Belay T.

By Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Nonliving characteristics of viruses:</li>
<li>They are acellular, that is, they contain no cytoplasm or cellular organelles; they carry out no metabolism on their own and must replicate using the host cell's metabolic machinery.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="viroids-virusoids-prions">Viroids, Virusoids, &amp; Prions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#viroids-virusoids-prions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Viroids are very small (200-400 nucleotides) circular RNA molecules with a rod-like secondary structure. They have no capsid or envelope &amp; are associated with certain plant diseases.</li>
<li>Virusoids are satellite, viroid-like molecules, somewhat larger than viroids (~1,000 nucleotides), which are dependent on the presence of virus replication for multiplication (hence 'satellite') - packaged into virus capsids as passengers.</li>
<li>Prions are infectious agents generally believed to consist of a single type of protein molecule with no nucleic acid component.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="essential-characteristics-of-viruses">Essential Characteristics of Viruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#essential-characteristics-of-viruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Size</li>
<li>25nm (picornavirus) to 250x350nm (smallpox virus).</li>
<li>Resolving power of a light microscope: 300nm, bacteria: 500–5000nm.</li>
<li>Genome</li>
<li>DNA or RNA: Double-stranded or single-stranded nucleic acid, depending on the species.</li>
<li>Structure</li>
<li>Viruses are complexes comprising virus-coded protein and nucleic acid; some viral species carry cell-coded components (membranes, tRNA).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="how-big-are-viruses">How big are viruses?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-big-are-viruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>A common mistake is that viruses are always smaller than bacteria.</li>
<li>While this is true in most cases, size alone does not serve to distinguish between them.</li>
<li>The largest virus particles (e.g. Granuloviruses) are 120-300nm in diameter &amp; 300-500nm long while the smallest bacteria (e.g. Mycoplasma) are only 200-300nm long.</li>
<li>Size alone does not differentiate viruses &amp; bacteria!</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-81e9546d.jpeg" alt="img-3.jpeg">
Comparative Sizes of Viruses and Bacteria
Fig. Different virus species are shown here to scale inside an E. coli bacterium</p>
<p>Page 5</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020
By Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Reproduction of viruses</li>
</ul>
<p>Takes place only in living cells. The virus supplies the information in the form of nucleic acids and in some cases a few enzymes; the cell provides the remaining enzymes, the protein synthesizing apparatus, the chemical building blocks, the energy, and the structural framework for the synthetic steps</p>
<ul>
<li>Antibiotics</li>
</ul>
<p>Viruses are unaffected by antibiotics, but can be inhibited by interferon and certain chemotherapeutic agents.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="morphology-and-structure">Morphology and Structure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#morphology-and-structure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A mature virus particle is also known as a virion. It consists of either two or three basic components.</p>
<ul>
<li>A genome of DNA or RNA, double-stranded or single-stranded, linear or circular, and in some cases segmented. A single-stranded nucleic acid can have plus or minus polarity.</li>
<li>The capsid: virus-coded proteins enclosing the nucleic acid of the virus and determining its antigenicity; can have a cubic (rotational), helical or complex symmetry is made up of subunits called capsomers.</li>
<li>Envelope: In some cases an envelope that surrounds the capsid and is always derived from cellular membranes.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-622c98a5.jpeg" alt="img-4.jpeg">
Virus Particle Structure</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="genome">Genome<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#genome" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>The viral genome is either DNA or RNA. Hence categorized as DNA or RNA viruses.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The nucleic acid of DNA viruses is usually double-stranded (ds) and linear or circular depending on the family;</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The nucleic acid of RNA viruses is usually single-stranded (ss), with the exception of the reoviruses, and is also segmented in a number of virus families.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Viruses with ssRNA are divided into two groups:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>If the RNA of the genome has the same polarity as the viral mRNA and can thus function directly as messenger RNA, it is called a plus-strand (or positive-strand) or "sense" RNA strand and these viruses are sense or plus-strand viruses</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>If the genome RNA has the polarity opposite to that of the mRNA, and therefore cannot be translated into proteins until it has first been transcribed into a complementary strand, it is called a minu strand (or negative-strand) or "antisense" RNA strand and the viruses are antisense or minus-strand viruses</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="capsid">Capsid<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#capsid" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The capsid is the "shell" of virus-coded protein that encloses the nucleic acid and is more or less closely associated with it.</li>
<li>The combination of these two components is often termed the nucleocapsid, especially if they are closely associated as in the myxoviruses.</li>
<li>The capsid is made up of subunits, the capsomers, the number of which varies but is specific and constant for each viral species.</li>
<li>These are spherical or cylindrical structures composed of several polypeptides.</li>
<li>The capsid protects the nucleic acid from degradation.</li>
<li>In all except enveloped viruses, it is responsible for the attachment of the viruses to the host cell ("adsorption,") and determines specific viral antigenicity.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="envelope">Envelope<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#envelope" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The envelope which surrounds the capsid in several virus families, is always dependent on cellular membranes (nuclear or cell membrane, less frequently endoplasmic reticulum).</li>
<li>Both cell-coded and viral proteins are integrated in the membrane when these elements are transformed into the envelope, frequently in the form of "spikes" (or peplomers,</li>
<li>Not all viruses have the envelope, and viruses can be divided into 2 kinds: enveloped virus and naked virus.</li>
<li>Enveloped viruses do not adsorb to the host cell with the capsid, but rather with their envelope.</li>
<li>Removing it with organic solvents or detergents reduces the infectivity of the viruses ("ether sensitivity")</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-8b1f799e.jpeg" alt="img-5.jpeg">
Fig. Acquisition of an envelope</p>
<p>Acquisition of an envelope occurs as the nucleocapsid buds out from the cytoplasm to the extracellular surface.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10) May 2010

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="spikes">Spikes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#spikes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>They arise from the envelope and are highly antigenic. These are glycoprotein projections which have enzymatic and/or adsorption and/or hemagglutinating activity.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-97f598d1.jpeg" alt="img-6.jpeg">
Figure 10.1 Microbiology Iris 12000 John Wiley &amp; Sons</p>
<ul>
<li>Influenza Virus glycoprotein spikes serve two basic functions</li>
<li>mediate attachment to cellular receptors which is essential for infectivity (hemagglutinin)</li>
<li>they act as enzymes (neuraminidase)</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-92ffab92.jpeg" alt="img-7.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="other-components-of-viral-particles">Other Components of Viral Particles<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#other-components-of-viral-particles" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="various-enzymes">Various enzymes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#various-enzymes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Viruses require a number of different enzymes depending on genome type and mode of infection. In several virus species, enzymes are a component of the virus particle, for example the neuraminidase required for invasion and release of myxoviruses.</p>
<p>Other examples include nucleic acid polymerases such as: RNA-dependent RNA polymerases in antisense viruses, DNA polymerases in smallpox viruses and RNA-dependent DNA polymerase ("reverse transcriptase") in hepatitis B viruses and retroviruses.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020</p>
<p>By Belay T.</p>
<p>Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Hemagglutinin</p>
<p>Some viruses (above all myxoviruses and paramyxoviruses) are capable of agglutinating various different human or animal erythrocytes. These viruses bear a certain surface protein (hemagglutinin) in their envelope that enables them to do this. The hemagglutination phenomenon can be used for quantitative viral testing or—in the hemagglutination inhibition test - for virus identification and antibody identification. In biological terms, hemagglutinin plays a decisive role in adsorption and penetration of the virus into the host cell.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="viral-structural-patterns-viral-symmetry">Viral Structural Patterns (Viral Symmetry)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#viral-structural-patterns-viral-symmetry" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="cubic-symmetry-rotational-symmetry">Cubic symmetry (rotational symmetry)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cubic-symmetry-rotational-symmetry" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Viruses with rotational symmetry are icosahedrons (polyhedrons with 20 equilateral triangular faces and 12 corners e.g. Adenovirus). The number of capsomers per virion varies from 32 to 252 and depends on the number of capsomers (two to six) making up one side of the equilateral triangle. The capsomers in a virion need not all be the same, either in their morphology, antigen make-up or biological properties. Purified icosahedral viruses can be crystallized, so that images of them can be obtained using the methods of radiocrystallography.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="helical-symmetry">Helical symmetry<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#helical-symmetry" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Helical symmetry is present when one axis of a capsid is longer than the other. The nucleic acid and capsid protein are closely associated in the ribonucleoprotein (RNP), in which the protein is tightly arrayed around the nucleic acid strand. This RNA-protein complex is known as the nucleocapsid, which takes the form of a helix inside the viral envelope. e.g. Influenza virus</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-b3f5d19b.jpeg" alt="img-8.jpeg">
Fig. Schematic structure of a myxovirus; virus with Helical Symmetry</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="complex-symmetry">Complex symmetry<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#complex-symmetry" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Complex structural patterns are found in phages and the smallpox virus. T-bacteriophages, for example, have an icosahedral head containing the DNA and a tubelike tail through which the DNA is injected into the host cell.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="capsid-symmetry">Capsid symmetry<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#capsid-symmetry" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-fa4068a4.jpeg" alt="img-9.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="classification-and-replication-of-viruses">Classification and Replication of viruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification-and-replication-of-viruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="learning-objectives">Learning Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#learning-objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>At the end of this Lesson, you will be able to:</p>
<p>Describe the classification schemes of viruses
Mention phases/steps of viral replication</p>
<ul>
<li>Explain important events in the phases of viral replication
Describe different processes in the replication of viral nucleic acids corresponding to the types and configurations of viral genome
Describe viral protein synthesis and control mechanisms</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="classification-of-viruses">Classification of viruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification-of-viruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The origins and evolution of the viruses are still largely in the dark. In contrast to the taxonomic systems used to classify the higher forms of life, we are unable to classify viruses in such evolutionary systems. An international nomenclature committee groups viruses according to various criteria and designates these groups, analogously to the higher forms, as families, genera, and species. Names used for viruses should be those approved by the International Committee on Taxonomy of Viruses (ICTV) and reported on the ICTV Virus Taxonomy website.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020</p>
<p>By Belay T.</p>
<p>Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>The International Committee on Taxonomy of Viruses (ICTV) is charged with the task of developing, refining, and maintaining a universal virus taxonomy.</li>
<li>This task encompasses the classification of virus species and higher-level taxa according to the genetic and biological properties of their members; naming virus taxa; maintaining a database detailing the currently approved taxonomy; and providing the database, supporting proposals, and other virus-related information from an open-access, public web site.</li>
<li>The ICTV web site (<a href="http://ictv.global" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">http://ictv.global</a>) provides access to the current taxonomy database in online and downloadable formats, and maintains a complete history of virus taxa back to the first release in 1971.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="baltimore-classification">Baltimore classification<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#baltimore-classification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The Baltimore classification clusters viruses into families depending on their type of genome. Unlike LUCA (Last universal common ancestor) for cellular organism, there is no presumed common ancestor for viruses. David Baltimore (nobel Prize 1975) proposed a classification based on the genome present in virions. Under the Baltimore system, the present virus classification comprises seven trees of life.</p>
<ul>
<li>DNA Viruses (Group I and II): dsDNA and ssDNA</li>
<li>RNA viruses (Group III, IV and V): dsRNA, ssRNA(+), ssRNA(-)</li>
<li>Retro-transcribing viruses (Group VI and VII): ssRNA (RT), dsDNA (RT)</li>
</ul>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 11), May 2020

Page 11</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="baltimore-system-of-classification">Baltimore system of classification<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#baltimore-system-of-classification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-dc127d33.jpeg" alt="img-10.jpeg">
Genetic material present in the virion</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-b48c48af.jpeg" alt="img-11.jpeg"></p>
<p>The taxonomic system used for viruses is artificial (i.e., it does not reflect virus evolution). Viruses are classified based on the following morphological and biochemical criteria:</p>
<p>i. Genome</p>
<p>DNA or RNA genome (important basic differentiation of virus types!) as well as configuration of nucleic acid structure: single-stranded (ss) or double-stranded (ds). RNA viruses are further subclassified according to plus and minus polarity.</p>
<p>ii. Capsid symmetry: cubic, helical, or complex symmetry
iii. Presence or absence of an envelope
iv. Diameter of the virion, or of the nucleocapsid with helical symmetry</p>
<p>Despite this element of "artificiality" in the system now in use, the groups appear to make biological sense and to establish order in the enormous variety of known viruses. See Tables below, Taxonomy of Viruses based on publications by the International Committee on Taxonomy of viruses.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020</p>
<p>By Belay T.</p>
