<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="replication-of-viruses">Replication of viruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#replication-of-viruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The 6 phases/steps in viral replication are as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>Adsorption of the virus to specific receptors on the cell surface</li>
<li>Penetration by the virus and intracellular release of nucleic acid (uncoating)</li>
<li>Proliferation of the viral components: virus-coded synthesis of capsid and noncapsid proteins, replication of nucleic acid by viral and cellular enzymes</li>
<li>Assembly of replicated nucleic acid and new capsid protein</li>
<li>Release of virus progeny from the cell</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-b1e0ddbe.jpeg" alt="img-12.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="adsorption">Adsorption<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#adsorption" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>It refers to the attachment of a virus to target cells. Virus particles can only infect cells possessing surface "receptors" specific to the particular virus species. For example, CD4 receptor for HIV; ICAM-1 receptor for rhinoviruses, the complement (C3) receptor that is also the receptor for the Epstein-Barr virus. It is the receptors on a cell that determine whether it can be infected by a certain virus. When a virus encounters such a cell, it adsorbs to it either with the capsid or, in enveloped viruses, by means of envelope proteins.</p>
<p>Practical consequences arise from the growing knowledge about the receptors:</p>
<ul>
<li>It aids in the development of antiviral therapeutics designed to inhibit the adsorption of the viruses to their target cells.</li>
<li>The genetic information that codes for certain receptors can be implanted into cells or experimental animals, rendering them susceptible to viruses to which they would normally be resistant. An example of this application is the use in experimental studies of transgenic mice rendered susceptible to polioviruses instead of primates (e.g., on vaccine testing)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="penetration-and-uncoating">Penetration and uncoating<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#penetration-and-uncoating" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Viruses adsorbed to the cell surface receptors then penetrate into the cell by means of pinocytosis (a process also known as viropexis). In enveloped viruses, the envelope may also fuse with the cell membrane, releasing the virus into the cytoplasm. Adsorption of such an enveloped virus to two cells at the same time may result in cell fusion. The next step, known as uncoating, involves the release of the nucleic acid from the capsid and is apparently</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020</p>
<p>By Belay T.</p>
<p>Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>(except in the smallpox virus) activated by cellular enzymes, possibly with a contribution from cell membranes as well.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="replication-of-the-nucleic-acid">Replication of the nucleic acid<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#replication-of-the-nucleic-acid" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Different processes are observed corresponding to the types and configurations of the viral genome.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="dna-viruses">DNA viruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#dna-viruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Replication of viral DNA takes place in the cell nucleus (exception: poxviruses)</li>
<li>Some viruses (e.g., herpesviruses) possess replicases of their own.</li>
<li>The smaller DNA viruses (e.g., polyomaviruses), which do not carry information for their own DNA polymerase, code for polypeptides that modify the cellular polymerases in such a way that mainly viral DNA sequences are replicated.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hepadnaviruses">Hepadnaviruses:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hepadnaviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>The genome consists of an ssDNA antisense strand and a short sense strand (Fig. e).</li>
<li>The infected cell transcribes an RNA sense strand ("template strand") from the antisense strand.</li>
<li>This template strand is integrated in virus capsids together with an RT DNA polymerase.</li>
<li>The polymerase synthesizes a complementary antisense DNA and, to "seal off" the ends of the genome, a short sense DNA from the template strand.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-85ba645c.jpeg" alt="img-13.jpeg"></p>
<p>Replication in Partially Double Stranded DNA Viruses (hepadnaviruses)</p>
<p>DNA replication in hepadnaviruses: by means of cellular transcription, a sense-strand RNA is made from the viral genome (antisense DNA, partially double-stranded) and integrated in the new virion, where a virus-coded RT produces new genomic DNA (−) and destroys the RNA.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="rna-viruses">RNA viruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rna-viruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Since eukaryotic cells possess no enzymes for RNA replication, the virus must supply the RNA dependent RNA polymerase(s) ("replicase").</li>
<li>These enzymes are thus in any case virus-coded proteins, and in some cases are actually components of the virus particle.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="single-stranded-rna">Single-stranded RNA<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#single-stranded-rna" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In sense-strand viruses, the RNA functions as mRNA “as is,” meaning the information can be read off, and the replicase synthesized immediately.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 11), May 2020. The document is available on</p>
<p>By Belay T.</p>
<p>Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Antisense-strand viruses must first transcribe their genome into a complementary strand that can then act as mRNA. In this case, the polymerase for the first transcription is contained in the mature virion and delivered into the cell.</p>
<p>In ssRNA viruses, whether sense or antisense strands, complementary strands of the genome are produced first (Fig. a, b), then transcribed into daughter strands. They therefore once again show the same polarity as the viral genome and are used in assembly of the new viral progeny.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="single-stranded-rna-replicaton">Single-stranded RNA Replicaton<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#single-stranded-rna-replicaton" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-a4318c5c.jpeg" alt="img-14.jpeg"></p>
<p>Single-stranded RNA viruses with sense-strand genome: the virus-coded RNA polymerase transcribes the viral genome (+) into complementary strands (-) and these into new genomic RNA (+). The latter is then integrated in the viral progeny.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-a42a8ba8.jpeg" alt="img-15.jpeg"></p>
<p>Single-stranded RNA viruses with antisense-strand genome: the RNA polymerase in the virion transcribes the viral genome (-) into complementary strands (+), which a virus-coded polymerase then transcribes into new genomic RNA (-)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="double-stranded-rna">Double-stranded RNA<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#double-stranded-rna" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A translatable sense-strand RNA is produced from the genome, which consists of several dsRNA segments (segmented genome). This strand functions, at first, as mRNA and later as a matrix for synthesis of antisense-strand RNA (Fig. c). Here as well, an RNA-dependent RNA polymerase is part of the virus particle.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020</p>
<p>By Belay T.</p>
<p>Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-e6a1ac2b.jpeg" alt="img-16.jpeg"></p>
<p>Double-stranded RNA viruses: while still in the partially decapsidated virus particle, the virus-coded polymerase transcribes complementary strands (+) from the antisense strand of the (segmented) double-stranded viral genome; these complementary strands are complemented to make the new double stranded viral genome.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="rna-replication-in-retroviruses">RNA replication in retroviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rna-replication-in-retroviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Retroviruses also possess a sense-oriented RNA genome, although its replication differs from that of other RNA viruses. The genome consists of two single-stranded RNA segments with sense polarity and is transcribed by an enzyme in the virion (reverse transcriptase [RT]) into complementary DNA. The DNA is complemented to make dsDNA and integrated in the cell genome. Transcription into sense-strand RNA is the basis for both viral mRNA and the genomic RNA in the viral progeny (Fig. d).</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10) May 2020. This document is available on</p>
<p>By Belay T.</p>
<p>Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-19bde433.jpeg" alt="img-17.jpeg"></p>
<p>RNA replication in retroviruses: the reverse transcriptase (RT) carried by the virion transcribes the viral genome (two sense-RNA strands) into complementary DNA (-), which is complemented to produce dsDNA and integrated in the cell genome. The viral RNA is first degraded. Cellular enzymes produce new genomic RNA (+).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="viral-protein-synthesis">Viral Protein Synthesis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#viral-protein-synthesis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="production-of-viral-mrna">Production of viral mRNA<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#production-of-viral-mrna" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In a DNA virus infection, cellular polymerases transcribe mRNA in the nucleus of the host cell from one or both DNA strands, whereby the RNA is processed (splicing, polyadenylation, etc.) as with cellular Mrna. An exception to this procedure is the poxviruses, which use their own enzymes to replicate in the cytoplasm. In viruses with antisense-strand, ssRNA and dsRNA, the transcription of the genomic RNA into mRNA is carried out by the viral polymerases, usually without further processing of the transcript. In sense-strand ssRNA viruses, the genome can function directly as mRNA.</p>
<p>Certain viruses (arenaviruses) are classified as "ambisense viruses." Part of their genome codes in antisense  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mo>−</mo><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(-)</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord">−</span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span> , another part in sense  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mo>+</mo><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">(+)</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 1em; vertical-align: -0.25em;"></span><span class="mopen">(</span><span class="mord">+</span><span class="mclose">)</span></span></span></span></span>  polarity. Proteins are translated separately from subgenomic RNA and the antisense-coded proteins are not translated until the antisense strand has been translated into a sense strand. Viral mRNA is produced for the translation process, based on both the genome of the invading virus and the nucleic acid already replicated. The actual protein synthesis procedure is implemented, coded by the viral mRNA, with the help of cellular components such as tRNA, ribosomes, initiation factors, etc. Two functionally different protein types occur in viruses:</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020</p>
<p>By Belay T.</p>
<p>Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>The "noncapsid viral proteins" (NCVP) that do not contribute to capsid assembly. These proteins frequently possess enzymatic properties (polymerases, proteases) and must therefore be produced early on in the replication cycle.</li>
<li>The capsid proteins, also known as viral proteins (VP) or structural proteins, appear later in the replication process.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="protein-synthesis-control">Protein Synthesis Control<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#protein-synthesis-control" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Segmented genomes: A separate nucleic acid segment is present for each protein (example: reoviruses).</li>
<li>mRNA splicing: The correct mRNA is cut out of the primary transcript (as in the cell the exon is cut out of the hnRNA) (examples: adenoviruses, retroviruses, etc.).</li>
<li>"Early" and "late" translation: The different mRNA molecules required for assembly of so-called early and late proteins are produced at different times in the infection cycle, possibly from different strands of viral DNA (examples, papovaviruses, herpesviruses).</li>
<li>Posttranslational control: This process involves proteolytic cutting of the primary translation product into functional subunits. Viral proteases that recognize specific amino acid sequences are responsible for this, e.g., the two poliovirus proteases cut between glutamine and glycine or tyrosine and glycine. Such proteases, some of which have been documented in radiocrystallographic images, are potential targets for antiviral chemotherapeutics (example: HIV).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="viral-maturation-morphogenesis">Viral maturation (morphogenesis)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#viral-maturation-morphogenesis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In this step, the viral capsid proteins and genomes (present in multiple copies after the replication process) are assembled into new, infectious virus particles. In some viral species, these particles are also covered by an envelope.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="release">Release<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#release" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The release of viral progeny in some cases correlates closely with viral maturation, whereby envelopes or components of them are acquired when the particles "bud off" of the cytoplasmic membrane and are expelled from the cell. In nonenveloped viruses, release of viral progeny is realized either by means of lysis of the infected cell or more or less continuous exocytosis of the viral particles.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="review-questions">Review Questions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#review-questions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Express the following terms related with viral replication: adsorption, penetration, uncoating, biosynthesis/synthesis, Assembly, Release</li>
<li>What determines host range in viral infections?</li>
<li>Mention the mechanisms through which viruses enter into or penetrate host cells.</li>
<li>Describe the mechanisms how new viruses are released from infected cells.</li>
<li>List the types of viral symmetry.</li>
<li>Write the highlight of Baltimore classification of viruses</li>
</ol>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10, May 2020)
This document is available on
by Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)
Page 19</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="viral-genetics">Viral Genetics<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#viral-genetics" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Like in higher life forms, viral genetic material is subject to change by mutation. The lack of a corrective replication "proofreading" mechanism results in a very high incidence of spontaneous mutations in RNA viruses. This results in greatly increasing the genotypic variability within each species ("viral quasispecies"). Furthermore, a potential for recombination of genetic material is also inherent in the replication process, not only material from different viruses but also from host cell and virus. This factor plays a major role in viral tumor induction and genetic engineering. Functional modifications arising from interactions between different viral species in mixed infections have nothing to do with genetic changes.</p>
<ul>
<li>e.g. phenotype mixing, interference, and complementation</li>
<li>Temporary nongenetic interactions between viruses in some cases may mimic genetic changes</li>
<li>Permanent genetic changes in viruses are caused, as in the higher life forms, either by mutation or recombination of genetic material.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="mutation">Mutation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mutation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Mutations are changes in the base sequence of a nucleic acid, resulting in a more or less radical alteration of the resulting protein.</li>
<li>So-called "silent mutations" (in the second or third nucleotide of a codon) do not influence the amino acid sequence of the protein</li>
<li>Medically important are mutants with weakened virulence that have retained their antigenicity and replication capabilities intact.</li>
<li>These are known as "attenuated" viruses.</li>
<li>They are the raw material of live vaccines.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="recombination">Recombination<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recombination" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>In cases where two different viral strains are replicating in the same cell, there is a chance that strand breakage and reunion will lead to new combinations of nucleic acid segments or exchanges of genome segments (influenza), so that the genetic material is redistributed among the viral strains (recombination)</li>
<li>New genetic properties will therefore be conferred upon some of the resulting viral progeny, some of which will also show stable heritability.</li>
<li>Genetic material can also be exchanged between virus and host cell by the same mechanism or by insertion of all, or part, of the viral genome into the cell genome.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="viruses-as-vectors-viral-vectors">Viruses as Vectors (viral vectors)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#viruses-as-vectors-viral-vectors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>The natural processes of gene transfer between viruses and their host cells can be exploited to give certain cells new characteristics by using the viruses as vectors.</li>
<li>Applications of viral vectors: Basic research, Gene therapy, Vaccines</li>
<li>If the vector DNA carrying the desired additional gene integrates stably in the host cell genome (e.g., retroviruses, adenoviruses, or the adeno associated virus), the host cell is permanently changed.</li>
<li>This can become the basis for "gene therapy" of certain functional disorders such as cystic fibrosis or parkinsonism.</li>
<li>Non integrating vectors (alphaviruses, e.g., the Sindbis virus, mengovirus, or vaccinia virus) result in temporary expression of a certain protein, which can be used, for instance, to immunize a host organism.</li>
</ul>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020
By Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>By this means, wild foxes can be vaccinated against rabies using a vaccinia virus that expresses a rabies virus glycoprotein.</li>
<li>Such experimental work must of course always comply with national laws on the release of genetically engineered microorganisms. It must also be mentioned here that only somatic gene therapy can be considered for use in humans.</li>
<li>Human germline therapy using the methods of genetic engineering is generally rejected as unethical.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="nongenetic-interactions">Nongenetic Interactions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#nongenetic-interactions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>In mixed infections by two (or more) viruses, various viral components can be exchanged or they may complement (or interfere with) each other's functions (phenotype mixing, complementation or interference).</li>
<li>Such processes do not result in stable heritability of new characteristics.</li>
<li>In phenotypic mixing, the genome of virus A is integrated in the capsid of virus B, or a capsid made up of components from two (closely related) virus types is assembled and the genome of one of the "parents" is integrated in it.</li>
<li>However, the progeny of such a "mixed" virus of course shows the genotype.</li>
</ul>
<p>In phenotypic interference, the primary infecting virus (usually avirulent) may inhibit the replication of a second virus, or the inhibition may be mutual.</p>
<ul>
<li>The interference mechanism may be due to interferon production or to a metabolic change in the host cell.</li>
</ul>
<p>In complementation, infecting viral species have genetic defects that render replication impossible.</p>
<ul>
<li>The "partner" virus compensates for the defect, supplying the missing substances or functions in a so-called helper effect.</li>
<li>In this way, a defective and nondefective virus, or two defective viruses, can complement each other</li>
<li>Example: murine sarcoma viruses for which leukemia virus helpers deliver capsid proteins, or the hepatitis D virus, which replicates on its own but must be supplied with capsid material by the hepatitis B virus</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="quasispecies">"Quasispecies"<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#quasispecies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When viral RNA replicates, there is no "proofreading" mechanism to check for copying errors as in DNA replication. The result is that the rate of mutations in RNA viruses is about <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>10</mn><mn>4</mn></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10^{4}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8141em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8141em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">4</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span>, i.e., every copy of a viral RNA comprising 10,000 nucleotides will include on average one mutation. The consequence of this is that, given the high rate of viral replication, all of the possible viable mutants of a viral species will occur and exist together in an inhomogeneous population known as quasispecies. The selective pressure (e.g., host immune system efficiency) will act to select the "fittest" viruses at any given time. This explains the high level of variability seen in HIV as well as the phenomenon that a single passage of the attenuated polio vaccine virus through a human vaccine recipient produces neurovirulent revertants.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="virus-host-interactions">Virus-host interactions<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#virus-host-interactions" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>☐ Possible consequences of viral infection for the host cell:</p>
<ul>
<li>Cytocidal infection (necrosis): viral replication results directly in cell destruction (cytopathology, so-called "cytopathic effect" in cell cultures)</li>
<li>Apoptosis: the virus initiates a cascade of cellular events leading to cell death ("suicide"), in most cases interrupting the viral replication cycle.</li>
<li>Noncytocidal infection: viral replication by itself does not destroy the host cell, although it may be destroyed by secondary immunological reactions</li>
</ul>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10, May 2020) This document is available on</p>
<p>By Belay T.</p>
<p>Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Latent infection: the viral genome is inside the cell, resulting in neither viral replication nor cell destruction.</li>
<li>Tumor transformation: the viral infection transforms the host cell into a cancer cell, whereby viral replication may or may not take place depending on the virus and/or cell type involved</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="cell-destruction-cytocidal-infection-necrosis">Cell Destruction (Cytocidal Infection, Necrosis)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cell-destruction-cytocidal-infection-necrosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Cell death occurs eventually after initial infection with many viral species. This cytopathological cell destruction usually involves production of viral progeny. Virus production coupled with cell destruction is termed the "lytic viral life cycle." Cell destruction, whether necrotic or apoptotic is the reason (along with immunological phenomena) for the disease manifested in the macroorganism</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="structural-changes-leading-to-necrosis">Structural changes leading to necrosis:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#structural-changes-leading-to-necrosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Morphological changes can often be observed in the infected cell.</li>
<li>The effects seen in virally infected cell cultures are well-known and are designated by the term "cytopathic effect" (CPE).</li>
<li>These effects can also be exploited for diagnostic purposes</li>
<li>They include rounding off and detachment of cells from adjacent cells or the substrate, formation of multinuclear giant cells, cytoplasmic vacuoles, and inclusion bodies. Inclusion bodies are structures made up of viral and/or cellular material that form during the viral replication cycle, e.g., viral crystals in the nucleus (adenoviruses) or collections of virions and viral material in the cytoplasm (smallpox viruses).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="shutoff-phenomena">Shutoff Phenomena<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#shutoff-phenomena" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Some viruses are able to block, more or less completely, steps in cellular macromolecule synthesis not useful to them. Such shutoff phenomena apparently contribute to rapid and efficient viral replication by eliminating competing cellular synthetic processes. These shutoff phenomena of course also have a pathogenic effect since they inhibit cellular metabolism, but not in such a way as to necessarily kill the host cell. Herpesviruses, for example, which possess DNA polymerase of their own, block cellular DNA synthesis. DNA replication in adenoviruses, by contrast, is directly coupled to that of the cell.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="apoptosis">Apoptosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#apoptosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Cells possess natural mechanisms that initiate their self-destruction (apoptosis) by means of predetermined cytoplasmic and nuclear changes. Infections with some viruses may lead to apoptosis. In rapidly replicating viruses, the viral replication process must be decelerated to allow the slow, energy-dependent process of apoptosis to run its course before the cell is destroyed by virus-induced necrosis. The body rapidly eliminates apoptotic cells before an inflammatory reaction can develop, which is apparently why virus-induced apoptosis used to be overlooked so often. Apoptosis can thus be considered a defense mechanism, although certain viruses are able to inhibit it.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="virus-replication-without-cell-destruction-noncytocidal-infection">Virus Replication without Cell Destruction (Noncytocidal Infection)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#virus-replication-without-cell-destruction-noncytocidal-infection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020
By Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>This outcome of infection is observed with certain viruses that do not cause any extensive restructuring of the host cell and are generally released by "budding" at the cell surface. This mode of replication is seen, for example, in the oncornaviruses and myxoviruses and in the chronic form of hepatitis B virus infection. However, cell destruction can follow as a secondary result of infection. If the immune system recognizes viral antigens on the cell surface, classifies it as "foreign" and destroys it.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="latent-infection">Latent Infection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#latent-infection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In this infection type, the virus (or its genome) is integrated in a cell, but no viral progenies are produced. The cell is accordingly not damaged and the macroorganism does not manifest disease. This form of infection is found, for instance, with the adenovirus group and in particular the herpesviruses, which can remain latent for long periods in the human body. Latency protects these viruses from immune system activity and thus is part of their survival strategy. However, a variety of initiating events can initiate a lytic cycle leading to manifest disease and dissemination of the virus. Repeated activation of a latent virus is termed recidivation (e.g., herpes labialis).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="tumor-transformation">Tumor Transformation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tumor-transformation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Infections by a number of viruses do not result in eventual host cell death, but rather cause tumor transformation of the cell. This means the cell is altered in many ways, e.g., in its growth properties, morphology, and metabolism. Following an infection with DNA tumor viruses, the type of host cell infected determines whether the cell reaction will be a tumor transformation, viral replication or lytic cycle. The transformation that takes place after infection with an RNA tumor virus either involves no viral replication (nonpermissive infection) or the cell produces new viruses but remains vital (permissive infection).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="carcinogenic-retroviruses-oncoviruses">Carcinogenic Retroviruses ("Oncoviruses")<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#carcinogenic-retroviruses-oncoviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="genome-structure-and-replication-of-the-oncoviruses">Genome structure and replication of the oncoviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#genome-structure-and-replication-of-the-oncoviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The genomes of all oncoviruses possess:</p>
<ul>
<li>gag (group-specific antigen),</li>
<li>pol (enzymatic activities: polymerase complex with reverse transcriptase, integrase, and protease), and</li>
<li>env (envelope glycoproteins) genes.</li>
</ul>
<p>These coding regions are flanked by two control sequences important for regulatory functions called LTR (long terminal repeats). These sequences have a promoter/enhancer function and are responsible for both reverse transcription and insertion of the viral genome into the cell DNA. Certain oncoviruses possess a so-called "onc gene" instead of the pol region (onc gene = oncogene, refers to a cellular gene segment acquired by recombination). These viruses also often have incomplete gag and/or env regions. Such</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 11), May 2010
This document is available on
BY
Stado
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>viruses are defective and require a helper virus to replicate (complementation). An exception to this principle is the Rous sarcoma virus, which possesses both an onc gene and a complete set of viral genes and can therefore replicate itself.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="genomic-organization-in-oncoviruses">Genomic Organization in Oncoviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#genomic-organization-in-oncoviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>a</th><th>LTR</th><th>gag</th><th>pol</th><th>env</th><th>LTR</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>b</td><td>LTR</td><td>(gag)</td><td>v-onc</td><td>(env)</td><td>LTR</td></tr><tr><td>---</td><td>---</td><td>---</td><td>---</td><td>---</td><td>---</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>a. Autonomously reproducing oncoviruses with the three replication genes: gag, pol, and env, flanked by the LTR regions.
b. Defective oncoviruses contain an onc gene instead of the entire pol region and parts of the gag and env regions.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="oncogenes">Oncogenes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#oncogenes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Over 100 oncogenes have been found in the course of tumor virology research to date. These genes enable tumor viruses to transform their host cells into tumor cells. The various types of oncogenes are designated by abbreviations, in most cases derived from the animal species in which the virus was first isolated. Further investigation of these viral oncogenes have now shown that these genes are not primarily of viral origin, but are rather normal, cellular genes widespread in humans and animals and acquired by the oncoviruses in their host cells, which can be transferred to new cells (transduction). Such a cellular gene, not oncogenic per se, is called a proto-oncogene.</p>
<p>The normal function of the proto-oncogenes concerns the regulation of cell growth in the broadest sense. Their gene products are growth factors, growth factor or hormone receptors and GTP-binding or DNA-binding proteins. Proto-oncogenes are potential contributors to tumor development that have to be "activated" before they can actually have such effects. This can occur by way of several different mechanisms:</p>
<ul>
<li>Chromosomal translocation: proto-oncogenes are moved to different chromosomes and thus placed under the influence of different cellular promoters, resulting in a chronic over expression of the corresponding protein.</li>
<li>Mutation of the proto-oncogene.</li>
<li>Transduction of the proto-oncogene by an oncovirus. The oncovirus promoter may induce over expression of the proto-oncogene, resulting in a tumor.</li>
</ul>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020</p>
<p>By Belay T.</p>
<p>Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="tumor-induction-by-oncoviruses">Tumor induction by oncoviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#tumor-induction-by-oncoviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Both types of carcinogenic retroviruses, i.e., those with no oncogene and intact replication genes (gag, pol, env, flanked by the LTR regions) and those that have become defective by taking on an oncogene, can initiate a tumor transformation. On the whole, oncoviruses play only a subordinate role in human tumor induction.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="retroviruses-without-an-oncogene">Retroviruses without an oncogene:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#retroviruses-without-an-oncogene" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>LTR are highly effective promoters. Since the retrovirus genome is integrated in the cell genome at a random position, the LTR can also induce heightened expressivity in cellular protooncogenes ("promoter insertion hypothesis" or "insertion mutagenicity"), which can lead to the formation of tumors. This is a slow process (e.g., chronic leukemias) in which cocarcinogens can play an important role. The transformed cells produce new viruses.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="retroviruses-with-an-oncogene">Retroviruses with an oncogene:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#retroviruses-with-an-oncogene" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A viral oncogene always represents a changed state compared with the original cellular proto-oncogene (deletion, mutation). It is integrated in the cell genome together with the residual viral genome (parts) after reverse transcription, and then expressed under the influence of the LTR, in most cases over expressed. This leads to rapid development of acute malignancies that produce no new viruses. Over production of oncogene products can be compensated by gene products from antioncogenes. The loss or mutation of such a suppressor gene can therefore result in tumor formation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="dna-tumor-viruses">DNA Tumor Viruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#dna-tumor-viruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Genes have also been found in DNA tumor viruses that induce a malignant transformation of the host cell. In contrast to the oncogenes in oncoviruses, these are genuine viral genes that have presumably developed independently of one another over a much longer evolutionary period. They code for viral regulator proteins, which are among the so-called early proteins. They are produced early in the viral replication cycle and assume essential functions in viral DNA replication. Their oncogenic potential derives among other things from the fact that they bind to the products of tumor suppressor genes such as p53, Rb (antioncogenes, "antitransformation proteins") and can thus inhibit their functions. DNA viruses are more important inducers of human tumors than oncoviruses (example: HHV8, papovaviruses, hepatitis B viruses, Epstein-Barr viruses).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="viral-pathogenesis">Viral Pathogenesis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#viral-pathogenesis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The term "pathogenesis" covers the factors that contribute to the origins and development of a disease. In the case of viruses, the infection is by a parenteral or mucosal route. The viruses either replicate at the portal of entry only (local infection) or reach their target organ hematogenously, lympogenously or by neurogenic spread (generalized infection). In both cases, viral replication induces degenerative damage. Its extent is determined by the extent of virus-induced cell destruction and sets the level of disease manifestation. Immunological responses can contribute to elimination of the viruses by destroying the infected cells, but the same response may also exacerbate the course of the disease.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10, May 2020)
