<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="transmission">Transmission<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#transmission" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Viruses can be transmitted horizontally (within a group of individuals or vertically (from mother to offspring). Connatal infection is the term used when offsprings are born infected.</p>
<ul>
<li>Table: Viral transmission patterns</li>
</ul>
<table><thead><tr><th>Pattern</th><th>Example</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Horizontal transmission</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Human—human (aerosol)</td><td>Influenza</td></tr><tr><td>Human—human (fecal—oral)</td><td>Rotaviruses</td></tr><tr><td>Animal—human (direct)</td><td>Rabies</td></tr><tr><td>Animal—human (vector)</td><td>Bunyaviruses</td></tr><tr><td>Vertical transmission</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Placental—fetal</td><td>Rubella</td></tr><tr><td>Mother—child (birth)</td><td>Herpes simplex virus (HSV), human immunodeficiency virus (HIV)</td></tr><tr><td>Mother—child (breastfeeding)</td><td>HIV, human T-cell leukemia virus (HTLV)</td></tr><tr><td>Germ line</td><td>In mice, retroviruses; in humans (?)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="portal-of-entry">Portal of entry<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#portal-of-entry" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>The most important portals of entry for viruses are the mucosa of the respiratory and gastrointestinal tracts.</li>
<li>Intact epidermis presents a barrier to viruses, which can, however, be overcome through microtraumata (nearly always present) or mechanical inoculation (e.g., bloodsucking arthropods).</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-b12ba551.jpeg" alt="img-18.jpeg"></p>
<p>Page 26</p>
<p>Viral dissemination in the organism</p>
<p>☐ There are two forms of infection:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Local infection</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>In this form of infection, the viruses spread only from cell to cell. The infection and manifest disease are thus restricted to the tissues in the immediate vicinity of the portal of entry. Example: rhinoviruses that reproduce only in the cells of the upper respiratory tract.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Generalized infection</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>In this type, the viruses usually replicate to some extent at the portal of entry and are then disseminated via the lymph ducts or bloodstream and reach their target organ either directly or after infecting a further organ. When the target organ is reached, viral replication and the resulting cell destruction become so widespread that clinical symptoms develop. Examples of such infection courses are seen with enteroviruses that replicate mainly in the intestinal epithelium, but cause no symptoms there. Clinical symptoms in these infections first arise in the target organs such as the CNS (polioviruses, echoviruses) or musculature (coxsackie viruses).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Another mode of viral dissemination in the macroorganism is neurogenic spread along the nerve tracts, from the portal of entry to the CNS (rabies), or in the opposite direction from the ganglions where the viruses persist in a latent state to the target organ (herpes simplex).</p>
</li>
</ul>
<table><thead><tr><th>Examples of Localized and Systemic Virus Infections</th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Virus</td><td>Primary Replication</td><td>Secondary Replication</td></tr><tr><td>Localized infections</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Papillomaviruses</td><td>Dermis</td><td>—</td></tr><tr><td>Rhinoviruses</td><td>Upper respiratory tract</td><td>—</td></tr><tr><td>Rotaviruses</td><td>Intestinal epithelium</td><td>—</td></tr><tr><td>Systemic infections</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>Enteroviruses</td><td>Intestinal epithelium</td><td>Lymphoid tissues, central nervous system</td></tr><tr><td>Herpesviruses</td><td>Oropharynx or genitourinary tract</td><td>Lymphoid cells, central nervous system</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10) May 2020
Page 27
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="organ-infections-organotropism">Organ Infections, Organotropism<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#organ-infections-organotropism" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Whether a given cell type can be infected by a given viral species at all depends on the presence of certain receptors on the cell surface. This mechanism explains why organotropism is observed in viruses. However, the tropism is only apparent; it is more accurate to speak of susceptible and resistant cells (and hence organs). Another observation is that cells grown in the laboratory in cell cultures can completely change their sensitivity or resistance to certain viral species compared with their organ of origin.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="course-of-infection">Course of infection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#course-of-infection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The organ damage caused by viruses is mainly of a degenerative nature; inflammatory reactions are secondary processes. The severity of the clinical symptoms depends primarily on the extent of virus-induced (or immunological) cell damage. Most of the viral progeny are produced prior to the occurrence of clinical symptoms, with consequences for epidemiology and antiviral therapies. Infections can go unnoticed if cell destruction is insignificant or lacking entirely. The terms inapparent, silent, or subclinical infection are used, in contrast to apparent viral infections with clinical symptoms. Virus replication and release do take place in inapparent infections, as opposed to latent infections in which no viral particles are produced.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="antibody-dependent-enhancement-of-viral-infection">Antibody-Dependent Enhancement of Viral Infection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#antibody-dependent-enhancement-of-viral-infection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Disease process can be worsened when viruses react with subneutralizing amounts or types of antibodies; antibodies exacerbate the infection. Fc fragment of the antibodies bound to the viruses can then react with the Fc receptors on specific cells. This makes possible for cell types to be infected that are primarily resistant to the virus in question because they possess no viral receptors (but in any case Fc receptors). ADE has been experimentally confirmed with a number of virus types to date includes herpes virus, poxvirus, reovirus, flavivirus, rhabdovirus, coronavirus, bunyavirus, and HIV species.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="virus-excretion">Virus excretion<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#virus-excretion" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Excretion of newly produced viruses depends on the localization of viral replication. For example, viruses that infect the respiratory tract are excreted in expired air (droplet infection). In generalized infections, not only the target organ is involved in excretion; the primary viral replication at the portal of entry also contributes to virus excretion. For example, enteroviruses replicate primarily in the intestinal wall are excreted in feces. Since the symptoms of a viral disease result from cell destruction, production, and excretion of new virus progeny precede the onset of illness. Patients are therefore contagious before they really become ill.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020
By Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="defense-mechanisms">Defense Mechanisms<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#defense-mechanisms" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Defense against viral infection can be classified in two groups; non-specific and specific defense mechanisms.</p>
<p>In nonspecific immune defenses, interferons play a very important part. Interferons do not affect viruses directly, but rather induce cellular resistance mechanisms (synthesis of “antiviral proteins”) that interfere with specific steps in viral replication.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="interferons-ifns">Interferons (IFNs)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#interferons-ifns" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Produced by most tissue cells when infected by a virus</li>
<li>Diffuses to uninfected cells and binds to surface receptors</li>
<li>stimulates macrophages and NK-lymphocytes</li>
<li>stimulates production of <strong>antiviral proteins</strong> which block viral replication</li>
<li>inhibits growth of virally infected cells</li>
<li>suppresses growth of tumor cells</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-f87f809a.jpeg" alt="img-19.jpeg"></p>
<p>The specific immune defenses include the <strong>humoral immune system</strong>, consisting mainly of antibodies, and the cellular immune system, represented mainly by the T lymphocytes. Cellular immunity is more important than humoral immunity. The cellular system is capable of recognizing and destroying virus-infected cells on the surfaces of which viral antigens are expressed. The humoral system can eliminate only extracellular viruses.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prevention">Prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The most important prophylactic measures in the face of potential viral infections are active vaccines. Vaccines containing inactivated viruses generally provide shorter-lived and weaker protection than live vaccines. Passive immunization with human immunoglobulin is only used in a small number of cases, usually as postexposure prophylaxis.</p>
<p>Chemoprophylaxis, i.e., administration of chemotherapeutic agents when an infection is expected instead of after it has been diagnosed to block viral metabolism, is now justified in selected cases, e.g., in immunosuppressed patients.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10, May 2020)
This document is available on
By Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)
Page 29</p>
<p>There are two basic types of vaccines:</p>
<ul>
<li>Active immunization</li>
</ul>
<p>The antigen (virus) is introduced into the body, either in an inactivated form, or with attenuated pathogenicity but still capable of replication, to enable the body to build up its own immunity.</p>
<ul>
<li>Inactivated vaccines</li>
</ul>
<p>The immunity that develops after so-called "dead vaccines" are administered is merely humoral and generally does not last long. For this reason, booster vaccinations must be given repeatedly. The most important dead vaccines still in use today are influenza, rabies, some flavivirus, and hepatitis A and B vaccines. Some inactivated vaccines contain the most important immunogenic proteins of the virus.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="live-attenuated-vaccines">Live attenuated vaccines<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#live-attenuated-vaccines" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>These vaccines confer effective and long-lasting protection after only a single dose because the viruses contained in them are capable of replication in the body, inducing not only humoral, but sometimes cellular immunity as well. Such live vaccines are preferable when available. Drawbacks and risks the increased potential for contamination with other viruses, resulting in more stringent testing and the possibility that a back-mutation could produce a pathogenic strain.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="vaccines-with-recombinant-viruses">Vaccines with recombinant viruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#vaccines-with-recombinant-viruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Since only a small number of (surface) viral proteins are required to induce protective immunization, viral vectors are used in attempts to express them in vaccine recipients. Suitable vectors include the least virulent virus strains among the picornaviruses, alphaviruses, and poxviruses. There must be no generalized immunity to the vector in the population so that it can replicate in vaccine recipients and the desired protein will at the same time be expressed. E.g. a rabies vaccine containing the recombinant vaccinia virus for use in animals.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="naked-dna-vaccine">Naked DNA vaccine<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#naked-dna-vaccine" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Since pure DNA can be inserted into eukaryotic cells (transfection) and the information it carries can be expressed, DNA that codes for the desired (viral) proteins can be used as vaccine material. The advantages of such vaccines, now still in the trial phase, include ease of production and high stability.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020
By Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Passive immunization</p>
<p>This type of vaccine involves the injection of antibodies using only human immunoglobulins. The protection conferred is of short duration and only effective against viruses that cause viremia.</p>
<p>Passive immunization is usually administered as a postexposure prophylactic measure, i.e., after an infection or in situations involving a high risk of infection, e.g., to protect against hepatitis B and rabies (locally, bite wound).</p>
<p>Chemotherapy</p>
<p>Inhibitors of certain steps in viral replication can be used as chemotherapeutic agents to treat viral infections. In practical terms, it is much more important to inhibit the synthesis of viral nucleic acid than of viral proteins. The main obstacles involved are: the low level of specificity of the agents in some cases (toxic effects, because cellular metabolism is also affected) and the necessity of commencing therapy very early in the infection cycle.</p>
<p>The Most Important Antiviral Chemotherapeutics</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Chemotherapeutic agent</th><th>Effect/indication</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Adamantanamin (amantadine)</td><td>Inhibition of uncoating in influenza viruses</td></tr><tr><td>Acycloguanosine (acyclovir, Zovirax)</td><td>Inhibition of DNA synthesis in HSV and VZV</td></tr><tr><td>Dihydropropoxymethylguanosine (DHPG, ganciclovir, Cymevene)</td><td>Inhibition of DNA synthesis in CMV</td></tr><tr><td>Ribavirin</td><td>Inhibition of mRNA synthesis and capping. Infections with Lassa virus and perhaps in severe paramyxovirus and myxovirus infections</td></tr><tr><td>Nucleoside RT inhibitors (NRTI)</td><td>Inhibition of RT in HIV (p. 454)</td></tr><tr><td>Phosphonoformate (foscarnet)</td><td>Inhibition of DNA synthesis in herpesviruses, HIV, HBV</td></tr><tr><td>Protease inhibitors</td><td>Inhibition of viral maturation in HIV</td></tr><tr><td>Neuraminidase inhibitors</td><td>Inhibition of release of influenza viruses</td></tr><tr><td>Antisense RNA</td><td>Complementary to viral mRNA, which it blocks by means of hybridization (duplexing)</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10, May 2020)
The document is available on
by Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)
Page 31</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="problems-of-chemotherapy">Problems of chemotherapy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#problems-of-chemotherapy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The close association between viruses and their host cells is a source of some essential difficulties encountered when developing virus-specific chemotherapeutics.</p>
<ul>
<li>Viral replication is completely integrated in cell metabolism</li>
<li>The virus supplies only the genetic information for proteins to be synthesized by the cell</li>
<li>Thus, any interference with viral synthesis is likely to affect physiological cellular synthetic functions.</li>
</ul>
<p>Specific intervention is only possible with viruses that code for their own enzymes (e.g., polymerases or proteases), which enzymes also react with viral substrates.</p>
<ul>
<li>Another problematic aspect is the necessity of administering chemotherapeutics early, preferably before clinical symptoms manifest, since the peak of viral replication is then usually already past.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="laboratory-diagnostic-methods-for-viral-infections">Laboratory diagnostic methods for viral infections<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnostic-methods-for-viral-infections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There are essentially three different diagnostic methods used in virology.</p>
<ol>
<li>Virus isolation by growing the pathogen in a compatible host; usually done in cell cultures</li>
<li>Direct virus detection in patient material; identification of viral particles using electron microscopy, viral antigens with the methods of serology, and viral genome (components) using the methods of molecular biology</li>
<li>Antibody assay in patient serum</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="virological-laboratory-diagnostics">Virological Laboratory Diagnostics<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#virological-laboratory-diagnostics" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Diagnostic approach</th><th>Methods</th><th>Detection/identification of</th><th>Advantages/disadvantages</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Isolation</td><td>Growing in cell cultures</td><td>Infectivity, pathogenicity</td><td>Slow but sensitive method</td></tr><tr><td>Direct detection</td><td>Electron microscopy, EIA, IF, hybridization, PCR</td><td>Viral particles, antigens, genome</td><td>Fast method, but may be less sensitive</td></tr><tr><td>Serology</td><td>EIA, IF, etc.</td><td>Antibodies</td><td>Retrospective method</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020
By Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="virus-isolation-by-culturing">Virus Isolation by Culturing<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#virus-isolation-by-culturing" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The virus is identified based on its infectivity and pathogenicity by inoculating in cell cultures with the specimen material. Certain changes observed in the culture indicate the presence of a virus cytopathic effect [CPE].</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="cell-cultures">Cell cultures<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#cell-cultures" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Viruses can be grown in many types of human or animal cells available for culture. Primary cell cultures can be created with various fresh tissues and can only divide a limited number of times. Cell lines can be developed from primary cultures with unlimited in-vitro culturing capacity. Example, HeLa cells (human portio carcinoma cells) and Vero cells (monkey renal fibroblasts). Viral replication in cell cultures results in morphological changes in the cells such as rounding off, formation of giant cells, and inclusion bodies (so-called CPE). The CPE details will often suffice for an initial approximate identification of the virus involved.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="sampling-and-transport-of-diagnostic-specimens">Sampling and transport of diagnostic specimens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sampling-and-transport-of-diagnostic-specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>☐ Sampling</p>
<p>Selection of suitable material depends on the disease and suspected viral species. Sampling should generally be done as early as possible in the infection cycle since viral replication precedes the clinical symptoms. Sufficiently large specimens must be taken under conditions that are as sterile as possible, since virus counts in the diagnostic material are almost always quite low.</p>
<p>☐ Transportation</p>
<p>The half-life of viruses outside the body is often very short. Transport must be arranged quickly under cold box conditions. A number of virus transport mediums are commercially available.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="laboratory-processing-of-the-material">Laboratory processing of the material<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-processing-of-the-material" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Before the host is inoculated with the specimen material for culturing, contaminant bacteria must be eliminated with antibiotics, centrifugation, and sometimes filtering.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="selection-of-a-host-system">Selection of a host system<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#selection-of-a-host-system" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The host system to be used is chosen based on the suspected (and relevant) virus infectors. Observation and incubation times, and thus how long a laboratory diagnosis will take, also depend on the viral species under investigation.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="identification">Identification<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#identification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Identification of the viruses is based first on the observed cell changes, and then determined serologically using known antibodies and appropriate methods such as immunoelectron microscopy, EIA, or the neutralization test. Methods that detect the viral genome are now increasingly used.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10, May 2020) This document is available on The document By Belay T. Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="significance-of-results">Significance of results<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#significance-of-results" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>It is an indicative of the etiology of the patient's disease. The most sensitive method of viral diagnostic detection, but it cannot detect all viruses in all situations. Another aspect is that the methods of virus isolation, with few exceptions, detect only mature, infectious virions and not the latent viruses integrated in the cells.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="amplification-culture">Amplification culture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#amplification-culture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The virus is grown for a brief period in a cell culture. Before the CPE is observed, the culture is tested using the antigen and genomic methods.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="direct-virus-detection">Direct Virus Detection<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#direct-virus-detection" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>It is to find the viruses directly in the patient material without prior culturing or replication. Viruses in serous fluids such as the contents of herpes simplex or varicella- zoster blisters can be viewed under the electron microscope (EM). It must be remembered, however, that the EM is less sensitive than virus isolation in cultures by a factor of  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><msup><mn>10</mn><mn>5</mn></msup></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">10^{5}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8141em;"></span><span class="mord">1</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord">0</span><span class="msupsub"><span class="vlist-t"><span class="vlist-r"><span class="vlist" style="height: 0.8141em;"><span style="top: -3.063em; margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="pstrut" style="height: 2.7em;"></span><span class="sizing reset-size6 size3 mtight"><span class="mord mtight"><span class="mord mtight">5</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span> . Viral antigens can be detected in secretions using enzyme immunoassay (EIA), passive agglutination, or in smears with immunofluorescence performed with known antibodies, for instance monoclonal antibodies. The viral genome can be identified by means of filter hybridization, or in smears or tissue sections with in-situ hybridization using DNA or RNA complementary to the viral genome as a probe.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="sampling-and-transport-of-diagnostic-specimens">Sampling and transport of diagnostic specimens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#sampling-and-transport-of-diagnostic-specimens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Transport of patient material for these methods is less critical than for virus isolation. Cold box transport is usually not required since the virus need not remain infectious.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="significance-of-results">Significance of results<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#significance-of-results" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A positive result with a direct virus detection method has the same level of significance as virus isolation. A negative test result means very little, particularly with EM, due to the low level of sensitivity of this method. The antigen assay and genome hybridization procedures are more sensitive than EM, but they are selective and detect only the viruses against which the antibodies or the nucleic acid probe used, are directed.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="virus-detection-following-biochemical-amplification">Virus Detection Following Biochemical Amplification<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#virus-detection-following-biochemical-amplification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Polymerase chain reaction (PCR) provides a highly sensitive test for viral genomes. First, nucleic acid is extracted from the patient material to be analyzed. Any RNA virus genome present in the material is transcribed into DNA by reverse transcriptase. This DNA, as well as the DNA of the DNA viruses, is then replicated in vitro with a DNA polymerase.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020</p>
<p>By Belay T.</p>
<p>Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="serodiagnosis">Serodiagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#serodiagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>If a viral infection induces humoral immunity, the resulting antibodies can be used in a serodiagnosis. When interpreting the serological data, one is confronted by the problem of deciding whether the observed reactions indicate a fresh, current infection or earlier contact with the virus in question. Two criteria can help with this decision. Detection of IgM (without IgG) proves the presence of a fresh primary infection. Concurrent detection of IgG and IgM in blood sampled somewhat later in the course of the disease would also indicate a fresh infection. It could, however, also indicate a reactivated latent infection or an anamnestic reaction.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="viruses-as-human-pathogen">Viruses as Human Pathogen<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#viruses-as-human-pathogen" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="course-outline">Course outline<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#course-outline" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-dna-viruses">1. DNA Viruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-dna-viruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="1-1-viruses-with-single-stranded-dna-genomes">1.1 Viruses with Single-Stranded DNA Genomes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-1-viruses-with-single-stranded-dna-genomes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>Parvoviruses</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="1-2-viruses-with-double-stranded-dna-genomes">1.2 Viruses with Double-Stranded DNA Genomes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-2-viruses-with-double-stranded-dna-genomes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>Papillomaviruses</li>
<li>Polyomaviruses</li>
<li>Adenoviruses</li>
<li>Herpesviruses</li>
<li>Poxviruses</li>
<li>Hepadnaviruses: Hepatitis B Virus and Hepatitis D Virus</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-rna-viruse">2. RNA Viruse<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-rna-viruse" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="2-1-viruses-with-single-stranded-rna-genomes-sense-strand-orientation">2.1 Viruses with Single-Stranded RNA Genomes, Sense-Strand Orientation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-1-viruses-with-single-stranded-rna-genomes-sense-strand-orientation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>Picornaviruses</li>
<li>Astroviruses</li>
<li>Caliciviruses</li>
<li>Hepatitis E Virus</li>
<li>Togaviruses</li>
<li>Flaviviruses</li>
<li>Coronaviruses</li>
<li>Retroviruses</li>
<li>Human Immune Deficiency Virus (HIV)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="2-2-viruses-with-double-stranded-rna-genomes">2.2 Viruses with Double-Stranded RNA Genomes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-2-viruses-with-double-stranded-rna-genomes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>Reoviruses</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h4 level="4" id="2-3-viruses-with-single-stranded-rna-genomes-antisense-strand-orientation">2.3 Viruses with Single-Stranded RNA Genomes, Antisense-Strand Orientation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-3-viruses-with-single-stranded-rna-genomes-antisense-strand-orientation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h4></div>
<ul>
<li>Orthomyxoviruses</li>
<li>Bunyaviruses</li>
<li>Arenaviruses</li>
<li>Paramyxoviruses</li>
<li>Rhabdoviruses</li>
<li>Filoviruses (Marburg and Ebola Viruses)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="3-subviral-pathogens">3. Subviral Pathogens<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-subviral-pathogens" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Viroids</li>
<li>Prions</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="learning-objectives">Learning Objectives<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#learning-objectives" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>At the end of the lessons, you will be able to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Describe the general properties of each virus</li>
<li>Discuss the pathogenesis and clinical features of each pathogenic virus</li>
<li>List and elaborate the Laboratory diagnosis for each pathogenic virus</li>
<li>Describe the epidemiology and prevention methods for each pathogenic virus</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="viruses-with-single-stranded-dna-genomes">Viruses with Single-Stranded DNA Genomes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#viruses-with-single-stranded-dna-genomes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The groups of viruses are contained in only one family, the parvoviruses, with only a single human pathogen type. This group's only human pathogen is parvovirus B19. The Geminiviridae, Circoviridae, and many other families have circular single-stranded DNA, but infect only plants and, more rarely, animals.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="parvoviruses">Parvoviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#parvoviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The parvoviruses are among the smallest viruses with a diameter of 19–25mm. They are icosahedral, nonenveloped, and their genome is in the form of single-stranded DNA (ssDNA). Some parvoviruses can only replicate in the presence of a helper virus (adenovirus or herpesvirus). Parvovirus B19, the only human pathogenic parvovirus identified to date, is capable of autonomic replication, i.e., it requires no helper virus. Some zoopathic strains also show this capability in rodents, dogs, and pigs.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture">Pathogenesis and clinical picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Parvovirus B19 replicates in the bone marrow in erythrocyte precursor cells, which are destroyed in the process. In patients already suffering from anemia (sickle-cell anemia, chronic hemolytic anemia), such infections result in so-called aplastic crises in which the lack of erythrocyte resupply leads to a critical shortage. In healthy persons, these infections usually run an asymptomatic course. They can, however, also cause a harmless epidemic infection in children, erythema infectiosum ("slapped-cheek syndrome" or "fifth disease".</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="this-boy-s-face-displaying-signs-of-erythema-infectiosum-or-fifth-disease">This boy's face displaying signs of erythema infectiosum, or fifth disease<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#this-boy-s-face-displaying-signs-of-erythema-infectiosum-or-fifth-disease" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-5a630ade.jpeg" alt="img-21.jpeg"></p>
<p>The symptoms of fifth disease are usually mild and may include:</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>fever</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>runny nose</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>headache</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>rash</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>This childhood disease, which used to be classified as atypical measles, is characterized by sudden onset of exanthem on the face and extremities.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Certain forms of arthritis are considered complications of a parvovirus B19 infection.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>The virus also appears to cause spontaneous abortions in early pregnancy and fetal damage in late pregnancy (hydrops fetalis)</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="epidemiology">Epidemiology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In the transmission route of human parvovirus B19, ddroplet infection or the fecal-oral route, analogous to other parvoviruses, is suspected. Parvovirus B19 spreads through respiratory secretions, such as saliva, sputum, or nasal mucus, when an infected person coughs or sneezes. It can also spread through blood or blood products. A pregnant woman who is infected with parvovirus B19 can pass the virus to her baby.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="prevention">Prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There is no vaccine or medicine that can prevent parvovirus B19 infection. No specific prophylactic measures are recommended. Chance of being infected or infecting others can be reduced by:</p>
<ul>
<li>washing hands often with soap and water</li>
<li>Covering mouth and nose while coughing or sneezing</li>
<li>not touching your eyes, nose, or mouth</li>
<li>avoiding close contact with people who are sick</li>
<li>staying home when you are sick</li>
</ul>
<p>Once the rash occurs, the cases are probably not contagious. Once you recover from fifth disease, you develop immunity that generally protects you from parvovirus B19 infection in the future.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="treatment">Treatment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#treatment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Fifth disease is usually mild and will go away on its own. Children and adults who are otherwise healthy usually recover completely. Treatment usually involves relieving symptoms, such as fever, itching, and joint pain and swelling.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="viruses-with-double-stranded-dna-genomes">Viruses with Double-Stranded DNA Genomes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#viruses-with-double-stranded-dna-genomes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Viruses with double-stranded DNA genomes are classified in six families:</p>
<ul>
<li>papillomavirus,</li>
<li>polyomavirus,</li>
<li>adenovirus,</li>
<li>herpesvirus,</li>
<li>poxvirus, and</li>
<li>hepadnavirus</li>
</ul>
<p>Carcinogenic types have been found in all groups except the poxviruses (See DNA tumor viruses).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="papillomaviruses">Papillomaviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#papillomaviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The papillomaviruses have a diameter of 55nm and contain an 8kbp dsDNA genome. There are two distinct regions within the circular genome. One that codes for the regulator proteins produced early in the replication cycle and another that codes for the structural proteins synthesized later. Over 70 papillomavirus types have been described to date, all of which induce either benign or malignant tumors in natural or experimental hosts.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture">Pathogenesis and clinical picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Papillomaviruses infect cells in the outer layers of the skin and mucosa and cause various types of warts by means of local cell proliferation. Specific virus types correlate with specific pathohistological wart types. Plantar and vulgar warts, flat juvenile warts, and juvenile laryngeal papillomas apparently always remain benign. By contrast, the genital warts caused by types 6 and 11 (condylomata acuminata) can show carcinomatous changes.</p>
<p>Of all papillomavirus-caused cervical dysplasias, 50% contain human papillomavirus (HPV) 16 and 20% HPV 18. All wart viruses induce primary proliferation of the affected cells with large numbers of viruses found in the cell nuclei. Whether a malignant degeneration will take place depends on the cell and virus type involved, but likely on the presence of cocarcinogens as well.</p>
<p>In carcinomas, the viral DNA is found in integrated form within the host-cell genome, whereas in premalignant changes the viral genomes are found in the episomal state.</p>
<p>Papillomaviruses possess oncogenes (E5, E6, and E7 genes) that bind the products of tumor suppressor genes: E6 binds the p53 gene product, E7 the Rb gene product.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Human papillomaviruses cannot be cultivated in vitro. They are detected and identified by means of histological analysis and, in malignancies in particular, by means of in-situ hybridization. Antibody assay results have a low significance level and these procedures are not standard routine.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10) May 2020

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="epidemiology-and-prevention">Epidemiology and prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemiology-and-prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Since viruses are produced and accumulate in wart tissues, papillomaviruses are transmissible by direct contact. Warts can also spread from one part of the body to another (autoinoculation). A certain level of prophylactic protection can be achieved with hygienic measures.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-23773576.jpeg" alt="img-22.jpeg">
Warts Caused by Papillomaviruses</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-6d0d8189.jpeg" alt="img-23.jpeg">
Genital warts</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-c4c87dc9.jpeg" alt="img-24.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="papillomaviruses-summary">Papillomaviruses summary<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#papillomaviruses-summary" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The over 70 viral types in the genus Papillomavirus are all involved in the etiology of benign tumors such as warts and papillomas, as well as malignancies, the latter mainly in the genital area (cervical carcinoma). These organisms cannot be grown in cultures. Diagnosis therefore involves direct detection of the viral genome and histological analysis. Serology is less important in this group.</p>

By Belay T.

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="polyomaviruses">Polyomaviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#polyomaviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The polyomaviruses can be divided into two groups:</p>
<ul>
<li>in one group are the SV40 and SV40-like viruses such as human pathogen JC and BK viruses</li>
<li>In the other are the true polyomaviruses such as the carcinogenic murine polyomavirus</li>
</ul>
<p>The designations JC and BK are the initials of the first patients in whom these viral types were identified. There are also a number of other zoopathic oncogenic polyomaviruses. The name polyoma refers to the ability of this organism to produce tumors in many different organs.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture">Pathogenesis and clinical picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The JC and BK viruses are widespread. Over 80% of the adult population shows antibodies to them, despite which, clinical manifestations like PML are very rare. The viruses can be reactivated by a weakening of the immune defense system. The JC virus attacks the macroglia, especially in AIDS patients, to cause PML, a demyelinating process in the brain with disseminated foci that is fatal within one year. The BK virus can cause hemorrhagic cystitis in bone marrow transplantation patients.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The JC and BK viruses can be grown in cultures, albeit with great difficulty and not for diagnostic purposes. Both can be detected with PCR. BK virus can be seen under the electron microscope in urine. Antibody assays are practically useless due to the high level of generalized contamination.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="epidemiology">Epidemiology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Despite the high level of generalized contamination, the transmission routes used by the human polyomaviruses have not been clarified. JCV and BKV have been found in sewage samples from different geographical areas in Europe, Africa, and the United States. This may indicate that ingestion of contaminated water or food could represent a possible portal of entrance of these viruses or polyomavirus DNA into the human population.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="polyomaviruses-summary">Polyomaviruses summary<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#polyomaviruses-summary" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A medically important polyomavirus, the JC virus, causes progressive multifocal leukoencephalopathy (PML), a demyelinating disease that has become more frequent as a sequel to HIV infections, but is otherwise rare. The same applies to the BK virus, which affects bone marrow transplantation patients. Electron microscopy or PCR are the main diagnostic tools.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="adenoviruses">Adenoviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#adenoviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Adenoviruses are nonenveloped, 70–90nm in size, and icosahedral. Their morphogenesis occurs in the cell nucleus, where they also aggregate to form large crystals. Their genome is a linear, 36–38kbp double-stranded DNA. Adenoviruses got their name from the adenoidal tissues (tonsils) in which they were first identified.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10, May 2020)
Page 41
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture">Pathogenesis and clinical picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Adenoviruses cause a variety of diseases, which may occur singly or concurrently. The most important are infections of the upper (sometimes lower) respiratory tracts, the eyes, and the intestinal tract.</p>
<ul>
<li>Infections of the respiratory tract</li>
<li>Infections take the form of rhinitis or abacterial pharyngitis, depending on the virus type as well as presumably on the disposition of the patient</li>
<li>They may also develop into acute, influenza like infections or even, especially in small children, into a potentially fatal pneumonia</li>
<li>The eye infections, which may occur alone but are often concurrent with pharyngitis, range from follicular conjunctivitis to a form of keratoconjunctivitis. May cause permanent partial loss of eyesight.</li>
<li>Intestinal infections: the primary gastroenteritis forms are caused by the viral strains 40 and 41, which are difficult to culture</li>
<li>Adenoviruses can persist for months in the regional lymph nodes or tonsils until they are reactivated.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In respiratory adenovirus infections, antibody assays in patient serum are the main approaches. Serology is unreliable in the eye and intestinal infections, since hardly any antibodies are produced in response to such highly localized infections. It is possible to isolate the viruses that cause respiratory infections by inoculating cell cultures with pharyngeal material or bronchial secretion and with conjunctival smears in eye infections. Enteric adenoviruses, on the other hand, are hard to culture. The best approach to detecting them is therefore to subject stool specimens to electron microscopy, enzyme immunoassay, or passive agglutination methods.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="epidemiology-and-prevention">Epidemiology and prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemiology-and-prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Humans are the source of infection. Generalized contamination of the population begins so early in childhood that adenovirus infections play a more significant role in children than in adults. Transmission of respiratory adenoviruses is primarily by droplet infection, but also as smear infections since the virus is also excreted in stool. Eye infections can be contracted from bathing water or, in the case of adenovirus type 8 in particular, iatrogenically from insufficiently sterilized ophthalmological instruments. The enteral infections are also transmitted by the fecal-oral route, mainly by contact rather than in water or food. Adenoviruses are the second most frequent diarrheal pathogen in children after rotaviruses.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="adenoviruses-summary">Adenoviruses summary<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#adenoviruses-summary" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>More than 41 types of adenoviruses and they cause a wide variety of diseases</li>
<li>Infections of the upper, less frequently the lower, respiratory tract and eye infections (follicular conjunctivitis, keratoconjunctivitis) are among the more significant clinical pictures.</li>
<li>Intestinal infections are mainly caused by the only not culturable virus types 40 and 41</li>
<li>Diagnosis: antibody assay in respiratory adenovirus infections.</li>
<li>Serology is not reliable in the eye and intestinal infections.</li>
<li>It is possible to isolate the pathogens in cell cultures from eye infections</li>
<li>Enteral adenoviruses are detected in stool by means of electron microscopy, enzyme immunoassay, or passive agglutination</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-cf521726.jpeg" alt="img-25.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="group-assignment-and-presentation">Group Assignment and presentation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#group-assignment-and-presentation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ol>
<li>Emerging and reemerging viral diseases</li>
<li>Subviral Pathogens</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="reading-assignment">Reading Assignment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reading-assignment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
