<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="interpretation-of-hepatitis-b-serologic-test-results">Interpretation of Hepatitis B Serologic Test Results<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#interpretation-of-hepatitis-b-serologic-test-results" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Hepatitis B serologic testing involves measurement of several hepatitis B virus (HBV)-specific antigens and antibodies. Different serologic "markers" or combinations of markers are used to identify different phases of HBV infection and to determine whether a patient has acute or chronic HBV infection, is immune to HBV as a result of prior infection or vaccination, or is susceptible to infection.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th></th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>HBsAg</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>anti-HBc</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>anti-HBs</td><td>negative</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>negative</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>negative</td><td>Susceptible</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>→ Consider vaccine</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>HBsAg</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>anti-HBc</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>anti-HBs</td><td>negative</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>positive</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>positive</td><td>Immune due to natural infection</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>→ Resolved HBV infection</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>HBsAg</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>anti-HBc</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>anti-HBs</td><td>negative</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>negative</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>positive</td><td>Immune due to hepatitis B vaccination</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>HBsAg</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>anti-HBc</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>IgM anti-HBc</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>anti-HBs</td><td>positive</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>positive</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>positive</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>negative</td><td>Acutely infected</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>HBsAg</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>anti-HBc</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>IgM anti-HBc</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>anti-HBs</td><td>positive</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>positive</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>negative</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>negative</td><td>Chronically infected</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>HBsAg</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>anti-HBc</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>anti-HBs</td><td>negative</td><td></td></tr><tr><td>positive</td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>negative</td><td>Interpretation unclear; four possibilities:</td><td></td></tr></tbody></table>
<ol>
<li>Resolved infection (most common)</li>
<li>False-positive anti-HBc, thus susceptible</li>
<li>“Low level” chronic infection</li>
<li>Resolving acute infection  |</li>
</ol>
<p>Adapted from: A Comprehensive Immunization Strategy to Eliminate Transmission of Hepatitis B Virus Infection in the United States: Recommendations of the Advisory Committee on Immunization Practices. Part I: Immunization of Infants, Children, and Adolescents. MMWR 2005;54(No. RR-16).</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-4054b1a5.jpeg" alt="img-33.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="department-of-health-human-services">DEPARTMENT OF HEALTH &amp; HUMAN SERVICES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#department-of-health-human-services" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</p>
<p>Division of Viral Hepatitis</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-939874b5.jpeg" alt="img-34.jpeg"></p>
<p><a href="http://www.cdc.gov/hepatitis" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">www.cdc.gov/hepatitis</a></p>
<p>Hepatitis B e antigen (HBeAg) is a marker of viral replication. When viral replication slows, HBeAg disappears, and antibody to HBeAg (anti-HBe) is detected. Anti-HBe may persist for years. The first antibody to appear is antibody to HBcAg (anti-HBc). Initially, it is of the immunoglobulin M (IgM) class.</p>
<p>■ Hepatitis B surface antigen (HBsAg):</p>
<p>A protein on the surface of hepatitis B virus; it can be detected in high levels in serum during acute or chronic hepatitis B virus infection. The presence of HBsAg indicates that the person is infectious. The body normally produces antibodies to HBsAg as part of the normal immune response to infection. HBsAg is the antigen used to make hepatitis B vaccine.</p>
<p>■ Hepatitis B surface antibody (anti-HBs):</p>
<p>The presence of anti-HBs is generally interpreted as indicating recovery and immunity from hepatitis B virus infection. Anti-HBs also develops in a person who has been successfully vaccinated against hepatitis B.</p>
<p>■ Total hepatitis B core antibody (anti-HBc):</p>
<p>Appears at the onset of symptoms in acute hepatitis B and persists for life. The presence of anti-HBc indicates previous or ongoing infection with hepatitis B virus in an undefined time frame.</p>
<p>■ IgM antibody to hepatitis B core antigen (IgM anti-HBc):</p>
<p>Positivity indicates recent infection with hepatitis B virus (≤6 mos). Its presence indicates acute infection.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10, May 2020)</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>by Belay T.</p>
<p>Download by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="epidemiology-and-prevention">Epidemiology and prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemiology-and-prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Humans are the sole reservoir of HBV. Transmission is parenteral, either with blood or body fluids containing HBV (sexual intercourse) that come into contact with mucosa, lesions, or microlesions in the skin. In transmission by blood, the tiniest amounts contaminating syringe needles, ear-piercing needles, tattooing instruments, etc. suffice to produce an infection. Hepatitis B infections from blood transfusions have been greatly reduced by thorough screening of blood donors for HBs antigens, despite which patients receiving multiple transfusions or dialysis remain a high risk group.</p>
<p>Another high-risk group includes all healthcare workers with regular blood contact. All blood samples must be considered potentially infectious and handled only with disposable gloves. Addicts who inject drugs with needles are also obviously exposed to a very high level of risk.</p>
<p>No effective chemotherapy against HBV has been developed to date. WHO recommends general hepatitis B prophylaxis in the form of active immunization with HBs antigen. In response to a sudden high-level infection risk (accidental inoculation with infectious material), persons whose immune status is uncertain should also be passively immunized with human anti-HBs antiserum—if possible within hours of pathogen contact.</p>
<p>It has not yet proved feasible to grow HBV in vitro. The antigen used in vaccinations can be isolated from human blood. Fear of AIDS infections has resulted in emotionally based, unjustifiable rejection of this vaccine. An alternative vaccine is now available based on developments in genetic engineering: the HBs antigen can now be synthesized by a yeast fungus.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="prevention-hepatitis-b-booster-vaccines">Prevention: hepatitis B booster vaccines<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention-hepatitis-b-booster-vaccines" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Periodic booster shots, especially for persons at high risk, were recommended for some time to maintain sufficient immune protection. However, since all successfully vaccinated persons build up immunity rapidly following renewed contact with the pathogen ("immunological memory"), this recommendation has been replaced in a number of countries by the following scheme:</p>
<ul>
<li>Following immunization on the classic model (0, 1, and 6 months), the anti-HBs antibody titer is measured within one to three months</li>
<li>Responders (titer 100 IU/l) require no booster</li>
<li>In hyporesponders and nonresponders (titer &lt;100 IU/l), an attempt should be made to reach a titer of 100 IU/l with a maximum of three further vaccinations.</li>
</ul>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020
By Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="rna-viruses">RNA viruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rna-viruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="viruses-with-single-stranded-rna-genomes-sense-strand-orientation">Viruses with Single-Stranded RNA Genomes, Sense-Strand Orientation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#viruses-with-single-stranded-rna-genomes-sense-strand-orientation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>✓ To date, six viral families are known: picornavirus, calicivirus, togavirus, coronavirus, flavivirus, and retrovirus</p>
<ul>
<li>Picornaviruses</li>
<li>Astroviruses</li>
<li>Caliciviruses</li>
<li>Hepatitis E Virus</li>
<li>Togaviruses</li>
<li>Flaviviruses</li>
<li>Coronaviruses</li>
<li>Retroviruses</li>
<li>Human Immune Deficiency Virus (HIV)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="picornaviruses">Picornaviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#picornaviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The picornaviruses are among the most thoroughly studied viruses of all. The name picorna is an abbreviation that stands for two characteristics of this family: they are small (pico) viruses with an RNA genome (rna). The RNA is polyadenylated at its 3′ end and has no cap at the 5′ end, but instead a virus-coded, basic protein, the VPg (virus protein, genome-linked), which functions as a primer for RNA synthesis.</p>
<p>Important human pathogenic genera of picornaviruses are:</p>
<ul>
<li>Enteroviruses: polioviruses, cocksackieviruses and echoviruses</li>
<li>Parechoviruses: types 1 and type 2</li>
<li>Hepatoviruses: hepatitis A virus</li>
<li>Rhinoviruses: common cold viruses (cause rhinitis)</li>
</ul>
<p>Enteroviruses (Poliovirus, Coxsackievirus, Echovirus) and Parechoviruses. The genus Enterovirus, isolated from the intestinal tract, includes</p>
<ul>
<li>Poliovirus (poliomyelitis pathogen) with 3 serotypes</li>
<li>Coxsackievirus, group A, with 22 serotypes</li>
<li>Coxsackievirus, group B, with 6 serotypes</li>
<li>Echovirus with 34 serotypes</li>
<li>Enteroviruses numbers 68–71</li>
</ul>
<p>The genus Parechovirus includes the species parechovirus types 1 and 2.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pathogenesis-and-clinical-pictures">Pathogenesis and clinical pictures<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis-and-clinical-pictures" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The enteroviruses and parechoviruses are transmitted per os and replicate at first in the lymphoid tissue of the pharyngeal space, later mainly in the intestinal wall. Then reach their</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10), May 2020
This document is available on
by Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)
Page 59</p>
<p>"target organs" via the bloodstream (e.g., CNS, muscles, heart, liver), followed by manifest organ infection, which, however, only develops in a small percentage of cases. Most infections run an asymptomatic course. Viremia is always present, so that even asymptomatic enterovirus and parechovirus infections confer effective immunity.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="enteroviruses-and-parechoviruses-clinical-syndromes">Enteroviruses and Parechoviruses: Clinical Syndromes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#enteroviruses-and-parechoviruses-clinical-syndromes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Virus type</th><th>Important syndromes</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Polioviruses</td><td>Poliomyelitis, paralysis, aseptic meningitis, encephalitis</td></tr><tr><td>Coxsackie viruses A &amp; B, echoviruses, enterovirus 68–70</td><td>Meningitis, paralysis, pharyngitis (herpangina), pneumonia, hepatitis, maculous and vesicular exanthems, including hand, foot, and mouth disease (HFMD)</td></tr><tr><td>Coxsackie virus B</td><td>In addition to the above: myalgia, pleurodynia (Bornholm disease), pericarditis and myocarditis, pancreatitis, diabetes</td></tr><tr><td>Enterovirus 71</td><td>Acute hemorrhagic conjunctivitis, HFMD</td></tr><tr><td>Parechovirus 1 and 2</td><td>Respiratory and gastrointestinal (“summer diarrhea”) infections</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>PCR or isolation of the virus is used. Specimens: CSF, pharyngeal smear, or lavage, with the best chances of success from stool. Serodiagnosis plays only a minor role.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="epidemiology-and-prevention">Epidemiology and prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemiology-and-prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Humans are the reservoir of the enteroviruses. Transmission is either direct (smear infection) or in food and water. Where hygienic standards are high, droplet infections also play a significant role. Special prophylactic measures to prevent infections with coxsackieviruses or echoviruses are neither practicable nor generally necessary. Salk introduced a dead vaccine in 1954 for poliomyelitis prophylaxis (IPV, inactivated polio vaccine) consisting of three poliovirus types inactivated by formalin. Five years later, the live vaccine (OPV, oral polio vaccine according to Sabin) was introduced, which contains three live but no longer neurovirulent poliovirus strains, either singly or in combination. The WHO plan to eradicate poliomyelitis worldwide would seem feasible with this vaccine as demonstrated by its eradication in several countries including all of South America.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020
By Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="hepatoviruses-hepatitis-a-virus">Hepatoviruses (Hepatitis A Virus)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hepatoviruses-hepatitis-a-virus" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>HAV differs in some characteristics from enteroviruses, to which group it was long considered to belong. Growth in cell cultures requires long adaptation. Only one serotype is known to date.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture">Pathogenesis and clinical picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The clinical picture of hepatitis A differs in no major particulars from that of hepatitis B. The disease nearly always takes a benign course. Only a small number of fulminant (and sometimes lethal) or chronic courses have been described. The pathogenic process at first corresponds to that of the enteroviruses, whereby hepatitis A replicates in the intestine and then, after a brief viremic episode, attacks its target organ, the liver. Disease manifestation with this pathogen, unlike most of the enteroviruses but similar to hepatitis B, involves immunological processes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>It is based on IgM detection due to the early presence of these antibodies in patient serum.</li>
<li>Lack of hepatitis A antibodies at the onset of clinical manifestations excludes hepatitis A.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="epidemiology-and-prevention">Epidemiology and prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemiology-and-prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Transmission is by food and water or in the form of smear infections.</li>
<li>Active immunization with an inactivated HAV vaccine is available.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="rhinoviruses">Rhinoviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rhinoviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>About 117 serotypes found to date. Their genomic organization and replication system generally match those of the enteroviruses. They differ in that they are acid-sensitive and slightly denser.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pathogenicity-and-clinical-picture">Pathogenicity and clinical picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenicity-and-clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The rhinoviruses are the causative pathogens of the common cold. Infect the mucosa of the nasopharyngeal space (nose and throat). They remain strictly localized there and do not cause generalized infections. In rare cases, mainly in children, they cause bronchitis or bronchopneumonia. The clinical picture is often worsened by bacterial super infection</p>
<p>Laboratory diagnostics are only required in special cases of rhinovirus infection. The viruses can be grown in cell cultures.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="epidemiology-and-prevention">Epidemiology and prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemiology-and-prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Rhinoviruses are transmitted directly by contaminated hands, and partly by droplet infection. Infective contacts between humans appear to involve mechanical inoculation (introduction into the nasopharyngeal space with fingers). Rhinoviruses occur worldwide, with pronounced proliferation in the winter months. The fact that everyone comes down with colds repeatedly is explained by the very brief immunity conferred by infection and the</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10) May 2020
The document is available on
by Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>many different viral types involved. Experiments have shown that the infections are always exogenous, i.e., not reactivations due to cold, wetness, etc. The only conceivable prophylactic measure is to avoid large groups of people.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="astrovirus-and-calicivirus-hepatitis-e">Astrovirus and Calicivirus; Hepatitis E<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#astrovirus-and-calicivirus-hepatitis-e" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Astroviruses and caliciviruses are enteritis pathogens in small children. Human pathogens in these groups include the Norwalk virus and hepatitis E virus (HEV). HEV is transmitted by the fecal-oral route, above all via drinking water, and causes relatively benign infections except in pregnant women. Hepatitis E is considered a traveler's disease.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="astroviruses">Astroviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#astroviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Astrovirus owes its name to its starlike appearance. It contains sense RNA with approximately 7500 nucleotides. It appears to have a replication strategy similar to that of the picornaviruses.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture">Pathogenesis and clinical picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Astroviruses that are animal and human pathogens are associated with episodes of diarrhea that nearly always run a harmless course. The etiological role of these viruses has still not been clarified. Astroviruses appear to possess only a low level of pathogenicity.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diagnosis-and-epidemiology">Diagnosis and Epidemiology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis-and-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Astroviruses are diagnosed by electron microscopy. They occur worldwide. They tend to infect young children and older persons weakened by other diseases.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="caliciviruses">Caliciviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#caliciviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Caliciviruses possess only one capsid protein and a polyadenylated, 7500-nucleotide RNA with a VPg at the 5' end. The surface of the viruses has a characteristic structure with small, regular, calyx like concavities that give the capsid the form of a Star of David. Caliciviruses are classified based on genomic similarities as either human caliciviruses (HuCV) or "small, round-structured viruses," SRSV. This designation stems from their initial identification under the electron microscope as "small, round, virus particles." The SRSV are grouped in two subtypes, I and II. Type I includes the Norwalk virus and a number of similar viruses named for their geographic venues, some with antigenicity differing from the Norwalk type</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-picture">Clinical picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Caliciviruses cause enteritis. Together with rotaviruses and adenoviruses, they are the most frequent viral enteritis pathogens in children. Often causing minor epidemics during the winter months ("winter vomiting disease")</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diagnosis-and-epidemiology">Diagnosis and epidemiology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis-and-epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Caliciviruses are diagnosed by EM or antigen assay in stool. Two-thirds of the adult population in the temperate zone carries antibodies to the Norwalk virus. SRSV are regularly implicated in minor epidemics and family outbreaks. The transmission route are fecal-oral route, water and uncooked foods.</p>

By Belay T.

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="hepatitis-e-viruses">Hepatitis E Viruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hepatitis-e-viruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>An infectious inflammation of the liver endemic to Asia, Central America, and parts of Africa</li>
<li>Apparently transmitted by the fecal-oral route</li>
<li>The RNA genome of the culprit agent has now been sequenced and the virus in question, the hepatitis E virus, has been classified with the caliciviruses</li>
<li>It occurs in at least 13 variants divided into three groups</li>
<li>In-vitro culturing of HEV has not succeeded to date</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture">Pathogenesis and clinical picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The clinical course of hepatitis E infections tends to be benign and resembles that of hepatitis A. It shows no chronicity. However, infections in the third trimester of pregnancy have a lethality rate of 10–40%.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Antibodies can be detected by means of an EIA. Due to cross-reactions with other caliciviruses, the specificity of the results is uncertain. A diagnosis is often arrived at based on clinical evidence and medical history (travel to endemic areas).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="epidemiology">Epidemiology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>HEV causes repeated outbreaks of considerable dimensions. The infections can be traced to contaminated drinking water. No specific prophylactic measures exist.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="togaviruses">Togaviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#togaviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The togavirus family (Togaviridae) comprises two genera. Alphavirus infections are transmitted by arthropods and are imported to central Europe mainly by travelers to tropical and subtropical countries. Their clinical pictures are variable, but almost always include joint pain (arthralgias). The most important representative of the genus Rubivirus is the rubella virus, the causative agent in German measles. This harmless childhood disease can cause severe embryopathies during the first trimester of pregnancy.</p>
<p>Possess an icosahedral capsid and a close fitting envelope. Genome is a single-stranded, polyadenylated, sense RNA. Replication not only produces new 40S genomic RNA, but a subgenomic 26S RNA fragment, which codes for the capsid proteins. Viral progeny are released by "budding" at the cell surface.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="togaviruses-include">Togaviruses include<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#togaviruses-include" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>zoopathic pestiviruses,</li>
<li>one species of rubivirus; rubella virus</li>
<li>alphaviruses with 25 species</li>
</ul>
<p>The alphaviruses most important to travelers are transmitted to humans by bloodsucking mosquitoes</p>
<ul>
<li>Chikungunya virus (Africa, Asia),</li>
<li>Sindbis virus (Africa, Asia, Australia),</li>
<li>Ross River virus (Australia, Oceania),</li>
<li>Mayaro virus (South America)</li>
</ul>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10, May 2020)
The document is available on
by Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture">Pathogenesis and clinical picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The arthropodborne alphaviruses, zoonoses of the tropical and subtropical regions, frequently cause asymptomatic or benign infections with fever, exanthem, and joint pain. Occasionally, however, persistent arthralgia and polyarthritis (lasting months or even years) do occur, sometimes involving joint destruction. Even rarer, sequelae include encephalitis and meningoencephalitis with high lethality rates. "German measles" is a harmless exanthemous infection in children and youths, caused by rubella virus, and transmitted by direct contact. The infections remain inapparent in nearly half the cases.</p>
<p>The virus, at first, replicates in lymphoid organs at the portal of entry and in the nasopharyngeal space, after which a viremia develops before the exanthem manifests.</p>
<p>In pregnant women, the virus takes this route through the placenta to the embryo, where it can cause congenital deformities or embryonic death, especially in the first three months of pregnancy. The organs in the developmental stage in this trimester are most seriously affected by the rubella infection. The most frequent congenital deformities are deafness, cataracts, cardiac defects, microcephaly, and spina bifida. In intrauterine embryo deaths due to rubella infections the immediate cause of death is usually myocardial damage. A measles infection confirmed by IgM detection or a raised antibody count is therefore an indication for a first-trimester abortion.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="diagnosis-and-prevention">Diagnosis and prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis-and-prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Serodiagnosis is the method of choice in suspected alphavirus and rubivirus infections. EIA methods are also available for IgM detection. There are vaccines to protect against alphavirus infections and rubella. The main aim of rubella prophylaxis is to prevent rubella-caused embryopathies. Since 10–15% of young adults are still susceptible to rubella infections and a live vaccine with few side effects that confers reliable immunity is available, serial vaccination of children (boys and girls!) is done before puberty. The vaccine is tolerated so well that prior immune status checks are not required.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="flaviviruses">Flaviviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#flaviviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Viruses in the flavivirus family (Flaviviridae) include the genera Flavivirus, Hepacivirus, and Pestivirus. Flaviviruses (the prototype being the yellow fever virus [Latin: flavus, yellow]) are transmitted by arthropods. They cause a biphasic infection that can have serious consequences (hemorrhagic fever with a high lethality rate). In southern and eastern countries, these viruses are significant human pathogens. Only one representative of this family, the tickborne encephalitis pathogen, is encountered in Europe.</p>
<p>Hepaciviruses (HCV and HGV) are not arthropodborne. HCV is transmitted mainly in blood (transfusions, blood products, intravenous drug use) and is a frequent cause of chronic disease (70% of cases), including cirrhosis of the liver and hepatocellular carcinoma. HGV is related to HCV and has not been characterized in detail as yet. Pestiviruses are only important in veterinary medicine.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020
By Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Flaviviruses show morphological uniformity, an icosahedral capsid and closefitting, spiked envelope. The genome of the flaviviruses is a single stranded sense RNA. It codes for three structural and seven nonstructural proteins. The morphogenesis of the virus occurs at the endoplasmic reticulum, into the lumen of which the finished viruses bud. These characteristics have not been directly demonstrated for the hepatitis C virus, which cannot be cultured in vitro. The pestiviruses cause severe animal epidemics (e.g., swine fever). They are not transmitted by arthropods.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="flavivirus-arthropodborne-yellow-fever-type">Flavivirus (Arthropodborne Yellow Fever Type)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#flavivirus-arthropodborne-yellow-fever-type" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The flavivirus family includes 63 species. Prototypic viruses of the family are the yellow fever virus, and the pathogen that causes European tickborne encephalitis (spring-summer meningoencephalitis, SSME).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="overview-of-the-most-important-flaviviruses-arthropodborne">Overview of the Most Important Flaviviruses (arthropodborne)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#overview-of-the-most-important-flaviviruses-arthropodborne" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Viral species</th><th>Transmitting vector</th><th>Geographic spread</th><th>Syndrome</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Dengue</td><td>Mosquito (Aedes, Stegomyia)</td><td>West Africa, Pacific, South and Southeast Asia, Caribbean, Venezuela, Colombia, Brazil</td><td>Dengue syndrome, DHF, DSS</td></tr><tr><td>Yellow fever</td><td>Mosquito (Aedes)</td><td>West and Central Africa, South and Central America</td><td>Hemorrhagic fever</td></tr><tr><td>Japanese B encephalitis</td><td>Mosquito (Culex)</td><td>East, Southeast and South Asia, western Pacific</td><td>Encephalitis</td></tr><tr><td>St. Louis encephalitis</td><td>Mosquito (Culex)</td><td>North and Central America, Brazil, and Argentina</td><td>Encephalitis</td></tr><tr><td>West Nile fever</td><td>Mosquito (Culex), ticks (Argasidae)</td><td>East and West Africa, South and Southeast Asia, Mediterranean countries, recently USA as well</td><td>Dengue syndrome, encephalitis</td></tr><tr><td>Tickborne encephalitis (Central European* and Russian)</td><td>Ticks (Ixodes)</td><td>Central Europe, Russia</td><td>Encephalitis</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul>
<li>Syn. spring-summer meningoencephalitis (SSME)</li>
</ul>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10, May 2020) The document is available on</p>
<p>By Belay T.</p>
<p>Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture">Pathogenesis and clinical picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The arthropodborne flaviviruses cause diseases of different levels of severity. The infections are typically biphasic.</p>
<p>An initial phase, not very characteristic phase, includes fever, headache, muscle pain, and in some cases exanthem (Denguelike disease). This phase includes a pronounced viremia. The illness, in this stage often not recognized as a flavivirus infection.</p>
<p>Secondary phase: initial phase may then be over or it may progress after one to three days to a second, severe clinical picture: a hemorrhagic fever with a high lethality rate involving hemorrhages and intravasal coagulation. In Dengue fever, this form is becoming more and more frequent and is called Dengue hemorrhagic fever (DHF) or Dengue shock syndrome (DSS) depending on the predominant characteristics.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A flavivirus infection always involves viremia (transmission by bloodsucking arthropods!). The viruses can be isolated from blood by inoculating cell cultures or newborn mice. In autopsies of fatal cases, the virus can be isolated from liver tissue. The viruses are labile by nature and identification can take time, for which reason the diagnostic focus is on serology (titer rise or IgM detection).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="epidemiology-and-prevention">Epidemiology and prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemiology-and-prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A cycle of infection involving a vertebrate host (mammals, birds) and a transmitting vector (bloodsucking mosquitoes and flies, ticks) has developed for most flavivirus infections. The cycles are efficient for the virus and relatively harmless for the host. The vertebrate host frequently shows few signs of disease and recovers from the infection after a brief viremia. During this period, the bloodsucking vector is infected, which thereafter remains a lifelong salivary secretor and thus infectious.</p>
<p>In ticks, transovarian transmission of the virus is also possible. The human host is a dead end for the virus, not a normal component of the cycle. Exceptions to this are Dengue fever and urban yellow fever. Humans are the only known main hosts for the Dengue virus.</p>
<p>There are two forms of yellow fever:</p>
<ul>
<li>Rural or jungle ("sylvatic") yellow fever with a monkey-mosquito-monkey (sometimes human) cycle.</li>
<li>Urban yellow fever with humans as the main hosts and Aedes mosquitoes as the transmitting vectors. This form is on the upswing due to increasing numbers of breeding places (e.g., empty tin cans in garbage piles) for the vector.</li>
</ul>
<p>Another "new" (more accurately: revived) infectious disease is the West Nile viral infection, observed for the first time in the USA (New York) in 1999, apparently introduced into the</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020
By Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>area by migrating birds. It is still not known why the geographic distribution of the virus or infected birds changed.</p>
<p>Vaccines are available against yellow fever (live vaccine) and European tickborne encephalitis (dead vaccine).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hepaciviruses-hepatitis-c-and-g">Hepaciviruses (Hepatitis C and G)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hepaciviruses-hepatitis-c-and-g" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>HCV was discovered by molecular biological in 1988. It is designated as "non-A-non-B (NANB) hepatitis". They belong to a flavivirus with approximately 10 kb sense RNA and several genotypes. HGV, another flavivirus, also causes hepatitis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture">Pathogenesis and clinical picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Hepatitis C resembles hepatitis B in many respects. One major difference is that it much more frequently produces a persistent infection (85%). In 70% of cases, develops into a chronic hepatitis which results in cirrhosis of the liver within 20 years and a hepatocellular carcinoma (HCC) in a further 10 years. The reason for the high level of viral persistence is thought to be a pronounced mutability facilitating evasion of the immune defenses - quasispecies of RNA viruses.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Antibody EIA using genetically engineered viral proteins and western blot to confirm the result. RNA can be detected by RT-PCR. Course of therapy monitored with quantitative PCR.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="epidemiology-and-prevention">Epidemiology and prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemiology-and-prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Transmission is by blood and blood products. High-risk persons include dialysis patients, healthcare staff, and needle-sharing drug consumers. Perinatal transmission is possible, but sexual contact does not appear to be a risk factor. The transmission route is not apparent in many cases, giving rise to the expression "community-acquired infection." Feasible protective measures are the same as in hepatitis B; no immunization by vaccine is available. Especially in combination with ribavirin, therapeutic use of interferon can lead to elimination of the virus in persistent infections and thus to prevention of cirrhosis of the liver and HCC.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="coronaviruses">Coronaviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#coronaviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The Coronaviridae family includes several viral species that can infect vertebrates - dogs, cats, cattle, pigs, rodents, and poultry. The name (corona, as in wreath or crown) refers to the appearance of the viruses. "Spikes", with club or drumstick-like swellings, are located at regular, relatively generous intervals on the pleomorphic envelope.</p>
<p>One coronavirus species (human coronavirus, HuCV) is known to be a human pathogen. It has at least two serotypes and probably a number of serological variants. In November 2002, a new coronavirus emerged in China and, after originally being mistaken as a new</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10) May 2020
The document is available on
BY
Stado
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>influenza recombinant, was identified as the causative agent of severe acute respiratory syndrome, or SARS, in spring 2003. Its origin, possibly from animals, is not known to date.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture">Pathogenesis and clinical picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Common cold-coronaviruses cause an everyday variety of respiratory infections, which are restricted to the ciliated epithelia of the nose and trachea. They are responsible for about 30% of common cold infections.</p>
<p>The immunity conferred by infection, apparently IgA-dependent, is short lived. Reinfections are therefore frequent, whereby the antigenic variability of the virus may be a contributing factor. Various enteral coronaviruses with morphologies similar to the respiratory types have also been described in humans. Their pathogenicity, and hence their contribution to diarrhea, has not been clarified.</p>
<p>The SARS virus is transmitted aerogenically with an incubation time of two to 10 days. Clinically, fever and a marked shortness of breath is noted, developing into a severe atypical pneumonia with new pulmonary infiltrates on chest radiography. Shedding of virus is by respiratory discharges. Whether the virus present in other body fluids and excreta plays a decisive role for virus transmission is not yet clear.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The common-cold coronavirus can be grown in organ cultures of human tracheal tissue or in human diploid cells. Isolating the viruses for diagnostic purposes is not routine. Serodiagnosis (complement-binding reaction, immunofluorescence or enzyme immunoassay) and electron microscopy are feasible methods. The SARS virus can be identified by PCR or isolated in the Vero cell line.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="epidemiology-and-prevention">Epidemiology and prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemiology-and-prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Transmission of the virus is by droplets, but close contact ("household transmission") with possibly other routes of transmission seems important. The only preventive measure to date is exposure prevention. Under therapy with ribavirin and intensive care, mortality of SARS is around 10%.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="reading-assignment">Reading Assignment!!!<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#reading-assignment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>SARS-CoV-2 (Aetiology of COVID-19)</li>
</ul>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020
By Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="retroviruses">Retroviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#retroviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Retroviridae family is the classification for all RNA viruses with reverse transcription of RNA to DNA in their reproductive cycles (RNA-dependent DNA synthesis). Only zoopathic retroviruses were known for many years. These viruses cause various kinds of tumors in animals. In 1980, retroviruses were also discovered in humans. This virus family includes seven genera, of which, three play significant roles in human medicine.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="human-retroviruses">Human retroviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#human-retroviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>HTLV-BLV retroviruses: HTLV types I &amp; II and the bovine leukemia virus. Spumaviruses, which only occur in animals, two of which are (probably) from humans. Lentiviruses with the human pathogens HIV 1 and 2, maedivirus (pneumonia), and visnavirus (encephalomyelitis) in sheep, viruses affecting goats and horses, and animal immune deficiency viruses.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="htlv-types-i-ii">HTLV types I &amp; II<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#htlv-types-i-ii" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A human pathogen retrovirus was isolated for the first time in 1980 from adults suffering from T-cell leukemias. It was designated as HTLV I (human T-cell leukemia virus). A short time later, a virus was isolated from hairy cell leukemia patients and named HTLV II. HTLV I is found in adults with T-cell malignancy as well as in patients with neurological diseases (myelopathies). HTLV II appears to be associated with T-cell malignancy and other lymphoproliferative diseases. Its own etiological role is still under discussion.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="human-immune-deficiency-virus-hiv">Human Immune Deficiency Virus (HIV)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#human-immune-deficiency-virus-hiv" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-4891790d.jpeg" alt="img-35.jpeg"></p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10) May 2020
By Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)
Page 69</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="types-of-hiv">Types of HIV:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-hiv" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>HIV-1  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mo>→</mo></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\rightarrow</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.3669em;"></span><span class="mrel">→</span></span></span></span></span>  related to SIVcpz (chimpanzee SIV) and HIV-2 (more closely related to SIVagm (African green monkey SIV)
HIV-1:</p>
<p>Three groups
M (referring for the main group), O (outlier), and N (non-M, non-O)</p>
<p>Group M:</p>
<p>nine subtypes (A-J) excluding I, which has been found to be recombinant between subtypes A and E) and
About 15 Circulating Recombinant Forms (CRFs)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="how-many-genes-does-hiv-have">How many genes does HIV have?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-many-genes-does-hiv-have" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>HIV has just nine genes (compared to more than 500 genes in a bacterium, and around 20,000-30,000 in a human). Three of the HIV genes, called gag, pol and env, contain information needed to make structural proteins for new virus particles. The other six genes, known as tat, rev, nef, vif, vpr and vpu, code for proteins that control the ability of HIV to infect a cell, produce new copies of virus, or cause disease. At either end of each strand of RNA there is a sequence called the long terminal repeat which helps to control HIV replication.</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-e779dddb.jpeg" alt="img-36.jpeg">
Genomic organization of HIV</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020</p>
<p>By Belay T.</p>
<p>Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="genes-essential-to-viral-replication">Genes essential to viral replication:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#genes-essential-to-viral-replication" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>tat gene: "transactive transcription," enhances the transcription and thus the expression of viral proteins by binding to the TAR (transactivation responsive region) in the LTR.</li>
<li>rev gene: posttranscriptional activator for splicing and transport of viral mRNA (production of structural proteins).</li>
<li>LTR sequence: promoter and enhancer elements.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="structural-genes">Structural genes:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#structural-genes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>gag gene: group-specific antigen.</li>
<li>pol gene: codes for the reverse transcriptase, a protease and the integrase.</li>
<li>env gene: envelope glycoprotein (gp).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="genes-not-essential-to-viral-replication">Genes not essential to viral replication:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#genes-not-essential-to-viral-replication" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Virus infection factor (vif): makes the virus more infectious.</li>
<li>"Negative" factor (nef): inhibits or activates viral transcription as required, influences T-cell activation, reduces CD4 expression.</li>
<li>vpr: controls rate of replication.</li>
<li>vpx: only in HIV 2, controls rate of replication.</li>
<li>vpu: only in HIV 1, contributes to viral release, increases CD4 turnover.</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-f888a31f.jpeg" alt="img-37.jpeg"></p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="hiv-replication">HIV replication<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hiv-replication" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>HIV can infect T-lymphocytes and other cells bearing the CD4 marker on their surface</li>
<li>The CD4 molecule is the main receptor for HIV, or more precisely for its gp120</li>
<li>In addition, either the chemokine receptor CCR5 (macrophage-tropic R5 HIV strains) or CXCR4 (T cell-tropic X4 strains) is used as a coreceptor.</li>
<li>☑ Persons with (homozygotic) missing CCR5 are highly resistant to HIV infection</li>
<li>A number of other coreceptors are also active depending on the viral strain involved</li>
<li>HIV is then taken in by the cell.</li>
<li>☑ After uncoating, reverse transcription takes place in the cytoplasm.</li>
<li>The rest of the viral replication process basically corresponds to the description of retroviral replication on "Introduction section"</li>
<li>The interaction of the many different contributing control genes is responsible for the long latency period and subsequent viral replication.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture">Pathogenesis and clinical picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>The pathogenicity of the disease is based on suppression of cellular immunity as a result of the loss of the CD4+ T helper cells</li>
<li>The primary infection either remains inapparent or manifests as "acute retroviral syndrome" with conjunctivitis, pharyngitis, exanthem, and lymphadenopathy, as well as a transitory meningoencephalitis in some cases</li>
<li>P24 antigen is detectable in serum after about 14 days, i.e., before the antibodies.</li>
<li>This stage is followed by a long period of clinical latency (the incubation period is described as 10 years), during which the carrier is clinically normal but may be infectious.</li>
<li>The HIV can persist in a latent state in CD4+ T lymphocytes, macrophages, and the Langerhans cells in the skin</li>
<li>Apparently, viral replication continues throughout this period, especially in lymphoid organs</li>
<li>The drop in CD4+ lymphocytes and the rise in the virus count (viral load) in peripheral blood is followed by the lymphadenopathic stage</li>
<li>Opportunistic infections then set in, frequently combined with lymphomas, the otherwise rare Kaposi sarcoma, or so-called AIDS encephalopathy (subacute AIDS encephalitis, AIDS dementia complex).</li>
<li>Similar neurological symptoms may also be induced because of HIV-induced immunosuppression</li>
</ul>
