<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="diagnostic-definitions-for-aids-in-adults">Diagnostic Definitions for AIDS in Adults<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnostic-definitions-for-aids-in-adults" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>CD4+ T cells/μl</th><th>Clinical categories</th><th></th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td>A</td><td>B</td><td>C</td></tr><tr><td>&gt;500</td><td>A1</td><td>B1</td><td>C1</td></tr><tr><td>200–499</td><td>A2</td><td>B2</td><td>C2</td></tr><tr><td>&lt;200</td><td>A3</td><td>B3</td><td>C3</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A3, B3, and C1–3 confirm AIDS diagnosis</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="clinical-categories">Clinical categories<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-categories" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A: Asymptomatic or acute (primary) HIV infection; persistent generalized lymphadenopathy (LAS)</p>
<p>B: Symptoms indicative of weakened cellular immune defenses, but no AIDS-defining diseases.</p>
<p>C: AIDS-defining diseases:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="aids-defining-diseases">AIDS-defining diseases<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aids-defining-diseases" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Viruses</p>
<p>HSV: chronic ulcer, esophagitis, bronchitis, pneumonia</p>
<p>VZV: generalized zoster</p>
<p>CMV: retinitis, encephalitis, pneumonia, colitis</p>
<p>JC virus: progressive multifocal leukoencephalopathy</p>
<p>HIV: encephalitis</p>
<p>HIV: wasting syndrome</p>
<p>Protozoans</p>
<p>Cryptosporidium: chronic diarrhea</p>
<p>Isospora belli: chronic diarrhea</p>
<p>Toxoplasma gondii: encephalitis</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="aids-defining-diseases">AIDS-defining diseases...<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aids-defining-diseases" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Bacteria</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Recurrent salmonellar septicemia</td></tr><tr><td>Recurrent pneumonia</td></tr><tr><td>Mycobacterial tuberculosis, pulmonary and extrapulmonary forms</td></tr><tr><td>Opportunistic mycobacteria (M. avium, etc.), disseminated or extrapulmonary</td></tr><tr><td>Fungi</td></tr><tr><td>---</td></tr><tr><td>Candida: esophagitis, pneumonia, bronchitis</td></tr><tr><td>Histoplasma, Cryptococcus neoformans, coccidiosis: extrapulmonary, disseminated</td></tr><tr><td>Pneumocystis carinii: pneumonia</td></tr><tr><td>Malignomas</td></tr><tr><td>---</td></tr><tr><td>Kaposi sarcoma</td></tr><tr><td>Invasive cervical carcinoma</td></tr><tr><td>B-cell lymphoma EBV-positive</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="laboratory-diagnosis">LABORATORY DIAGNOSIS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#laboratory-diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Serology (ELISA and rapid test algorithms). IgG develops 4-6 weeks post exposure and remains detectable for life. Its presence in serum therefore indicates infection. Exception: uninfected infants of HIV-positive mothers.</li>
<li>Direct detection of virus</li>
<li>p24 antigen ELISA</li>
<li>Cell culture</li>
<li>PCR – to determine viral load</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="prevention">Prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Exposure prophylaxis when contact with blood is involved (drug addicts, healthcare staff) and sexual intercourse. Postexposure prophylaxis and prophylaxis in pregnancy with chemotherapeutics can be used.</p>
<p>Standard vaccines can be used to prevent other infections.</p>
<ul>
<li>For example, opportunistic infections in HIV-positive persons, especially children showing no symptoms.</li>
<li>The dead vaccine type is recommended for polio.</li>
<li>Live vaccine materials should generally not be used in persons showing AIDS symptoms.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="therapy">Therapy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#therapy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Commonly used antiretroviral drug classes:</li>
<li>Nucleoside analogues (against Reverse Transcriptase)</li>
<li>Non-Nucleoside analogues (against Reverse Transcriptase)</li>
<li>Protease Inhibitors</li>
<li>Fusion Inhibitors (against the gp41; recently introduced; not widely used)</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="antiretroviral-mode-of-action">Antiretroviral mode of action<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#antiretroviral-mode-of-action" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Nucleoside reverse transcriptase inhibitors</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Nucleoside analogue lead to premature termination of HIV DNA chain</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Not recommended as monotherapy – single drug regimen leads to resistance</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Non-nucleoside reverse transcriptase inhibitors</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Non-nucleoside analogue Lead to premature termination of HIV DNA chain</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="protease-inhibitors-pis">Protease inhibitors (PIs)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#protease-inhibitors-pis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>HIV protease enzyme</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>cleaves various polyproteins in the process of producing mature infectious virions</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Protease Inhibitors or PIs</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>interfere with the production of HIV protease</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>lead to reduction of the virus in the body</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>reduction is sometimes significant enough to lead to undetectable levels of virus</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>do not use PIs alone (monotherapy) because rapid resistance will develop—they should be used in combination with other drugs</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="drug-resistance">Drug resistance<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#drug-resistance" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Drug resistance development is a common phenomenon among drug experienced patients. HIV develops resistance against all class of ARV drugs. It does this by undergoing genetic changes on the genes coding for target proteins so that they escape from the drug effect. Drug resistant variants escape drug activity via different mechanisms: changing the gp41 protein sequence for fusion inhibitors.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="impairing-the-active-pocket-allosteric-site-for-nnrti">Impairing the active pocket (allosteric site) for NNRTI<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#impairing-the-active-pocket-allosteric-site-for-nnrti" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="combination-treatments-of-hiv-infections">Combination Treatments of HIV Infections:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#combination-treatments-of-hiv-infections" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>To avoid development of resistant HIV variants, a combination of at least three drugs from at least two substance classes is usually administered. The following combinations are currently established practice:</p>
<p>a) One PI and two NRTIs
b) One NNRTI and two NRTIs
c) Two PIs and one or two NRTIs
d) One PI and one NNRTI, alternatively with one or two NRTIs as well;
e) Three NRTIs
a) and b) appear to produce the best long-term results.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="viruses-with-ds-rna-genomes-reoviruses">Viruses with ds-RNA Genomes: Reoviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#viruses-with-ds-rna-genomes-reoviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The name reovirus is derived from the abbreviation for respiratory enteric orphan virus, recalling that no diseases were associated with the virus upon its discovery (hence "orphan virus"). All of the viruses in the family Reoviridae possess a double icosahedral capsid. It contains the segmented RNA genome, comprising from 10 to 12 double-stranded subunits depending on the species.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="family-reoviridae">Family Reoviridae<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#family-reoviridae" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The family Reoviridae includes, in addition to phytopathogenic and zoopathogenic strains, three genera in which human pathogens are classified:</p>
<ul>
<li>Coltiviruses include a large number of pathogens significant in veterinary medicine as well as the human pathogen virus that causes Colorado tick fever</li>
<li>Reoviruses in the narrower sense, with three serogroups</li>
<li>Rotaviruses, groups A to F, further subdivided into subgroups, serotypes, and electropherotypes</li>
</ul>
<p>The rotaviral genome consists of eleven segments of double-stranded RNA. Each segment codes for one viral protein. Some segments in other reovirses code for two or three proteins.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture">Pathogenesis and clinical picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Coltiviruses:</li>
</ul>
<p>Colorado tick fever usually runs a mild course with fever, myalgias, nausea, and vomiting, rarely encephalitis</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10) May 2010
The document is available on
by Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)
Page 79</p>
<ul>
<li>Reoviruses</li>
</ul>
<p>Implication in diseases is still uncertain. It appears they are capable of infecting the respiratory and intestinal tracts of children. The fact that they are also found very frequently in asymptomatic persons makes it difficult to correlate them with specific clinical pictures.</p>
<ul>
<li>Rotaviruses</li>
</ul>
<p>In the mid-seventies these viruses were recognized as diarrhea- causing viruses in infants and small children. They are the most frequent cause of diarrhea in children aged six months to two years. It was recently discovered that they also play a role in infections of the elderly, and above all in immunosuppressed patients (e.g., bone marrow transplant patients), and can cause severe clinical pictures in these groups. Rotaviruses enter the body per os or by droplet infection, replicate in the villi of the small intestine and cause diarrhea, potentially resulting in exsiccosis.</p>
<ul>
<li>Diagnosis</li>
</ul>
<p>Colorado tick fever can be diagnosed serologically. Reovirus infections can be diagnosed by isolating in cell cultures.</p>
<p>Rotaviruses do not readily grow in cell cultures for diagnostic purposes. They can be detected more readily under an electron microscope or in antigen assays using commercially available solid phase tests (EIA) or passive agglutination. An elegant typing method for the different rotavirus strains involves analysis of the electrophoretic mobility of the 11 dsRNA strands of the viral genome.</p>
<ul>
<li>Epidemiology</li>
</ul>
<p>Humans are the sole natural reservoir of rotaviruses. Generalized contamination is practically 100% when children reach school age, but carriers and reinfections are still possible despite immunity. Diarrheal infections are among the most important causes of death in small children in developing countries; 20% of these infections are due to rotaviruses. Rotaviruses remain viable for long periods on objects and skin (hands!) and are therefore spread rapidly by infected persons and healthy carriers. The most effective prophylactic approach is to practice stringent hygiene.</p>
<ul>
<li>Rotavirus vaccines</li>
</ul>
<p>The vaccine is very effective in preventing rotavirus gastroenteritis. CDC recommends routine vaccination of infants with either of the two available vaccines:</p>
<ul>
<li>RotaTeq® (RV5), which is given in 3 doses at ages 2 months, 4 months, and 6 months; or</li>
<li>Rotarix® (RV1), which is given in 2 doses at ages 2 months and 4 months</li>
</ul>
<p>Both rotavirus vaccines are given orally. The vaccines are very effective (85% to 98%) in preventing severe rotavirus disease. Rotavirus vaccines will not prevent diarrhea or vomiting caused by other viruses, but they are very effective against rotavirus infection.</p>

By Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="viruses-with-single-stranded-rna-genomes-antisense-strand-orientation">Viruses with Single-Stranded RNA Genomes, Antisense-Strand Orientation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#viruses-with-single-stranded-rna-genomes-antisense-strand-orientation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Six viral families have an antisense RNA genome:</li>
<li>Orthomyxoviridae,</li>
<li>Bunyaviridae,</li>
<li>Arenaviridae,</li>
<li>Paramyxoviridae,</li>
<li>Rhabdoviridae,</li>
<li>Filoviridae</li>
</ul>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10) May 2020
The document is available on
by Belay T.

Page 81</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="orthomyxoviruses">Orthomyxoviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#orthomyxoviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The family has one genus, Influenza virus. Influenza virus has three types: influenza A, B, and C. Influenza A is the most important and most frequently observed influenza virus. It repeatedly causes epidemics and even pandemics at greater intervals. In contrast, influenza B tends to persist in endemic form and causes few outbreaks. Influenza C is rarely isolated, most frequently in youths. It plays on a minor role as an infective pathogen.</p>
<table><thead><tr><th></th><th>A</th><th>B</th><th>C</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Severity of illness</td><td>++++</td><td>++</td><td>+</td></tr><tr><td>Subtypes</td><td>Yes</td><td>No</td><td>No</td></tr><tr><td>Animal reservoir</td><td>Yes</td><td>No</td><td>No</td></tr><tr><td>Spread in humans</td><td>Pandemic</td><td>Epidemic</td><td>Sporadic</td></tr><tr><td>Antigenic changes</td><td>Shift, drift</td><td>Drift</td><td>Drift</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="structure">Structure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#structure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>All influenza viruses show the same and a pronounced pleomorphism. The genome of the influenza viruses is segmented and comprises eight separate antisenses RNA strands each of which codes for one specific protein. Together with the nucleoprotein, they form the helical nucleocapsid. Closely association with this structure is the RNA polymerase complex, which consists of three high-molecular-weight proteins with different functions. The nucleocapsid itself is embedded in a protein (so-called membrane or matrix protein). The virus is enclosed by an envelope made of cell membrane lipids with viral protein inclusions (hemagglutinin and neuraminidase, responsible for infectivity and viral progeny release). Both proteins are seen under the electron microscope as protrusions ("spikes") on the virus surface.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020
By Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Influenza A Virus Structure</p>
<ul>
<li>Hemagglutinin (HA)</li>
<li>Receptor binding (sialic acid)</li>
<li>Membrane fusion</li>
<li>Neutralizing antibody target</li>
<li>Neuraminidase (NA)</li>
<li>Remove sialic acid residues</li>
<li>Virion release</li>
<li>Ion channel (M2)</li>
<li>H⁺-dependent uncoating</li>
<li>Influenza A only</li>
<li>Influenza A subtypes based on HA (16) and NA (9)</li>
<li>H1N1, H3N2</li>
</ul>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-6f63203e.jpeg" alt="img-44.jpeg"></p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="influenza-antigenic-variation">Influenza Antigenic Variation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#influenza-antigenic-variation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="antigenic-drift">Antigenic drift<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#antigenic-drift" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Occurs with influenza A, B, and C. Small number of slowly occurring changes (mutations); error-prone viral RNA polymerase. HA changes most prominent, but can occur in any viral gene. It is partially responsible for yearly vaccine changes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="antigenic-shift">Antigenic shift<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#antigenic-shift" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Influenza A only</li>
<li>Large dramatic changes that occur rapidly</li>
<li>Primarily responsible for pandemics</li>
<li>Due to gene shuffling and reassortment</li>
<li>Requirements:</li>
<li>Segmented genome</li>
<li>Multiple HA and NA subtypes</li>
<li>Animal reservoir (wild aquatic birds)</li>
<li>Susceptible species for both avian and human influenza (swine)</li>
</ul>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10, May 2020)</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pathways-for-generation-of-virulent-pandemic-influenza-viruses">Pathways for generation of virulent pandemic influenza viruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathways-for-generation-of-virulent-pandemic-influenza-viruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Human influenza virus</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-0e892d73.jpeg" alt="img-45.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-06fe0f8d.jpeg" alt="img-46.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-6c7e3245.jpeg" alt="img-47.jpeg">
Avian influenza virus</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-6ea7a682.jpeg" alt="img-48.jpeg"></p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-8ccf68a9.jpeg" alt="img-49.jpeg">
Swine</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture">Pathogenesis and clinical picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Aerogenically transmitted influenza viruses normally replicate in the mucosa of the nasopharynx, resulting in a pharyngitis or at most a tracheobronchitis, after an IP of 24-72 hours. Pulmonary dissemination of the infection can result from an upper respiratory infection or manifest without one, whereby the prognosis in the latter case is less favorable. Pneumonia caused solely by the influenza virus is rare. As a rule, bacterial superinfections with staphylococci, streptococci, pneumococci, or Haemophilus bacteria are responsible. These infections, which used to be the normal cause of influenza deaths (Haemophilus influenzae in the "Spanish flu" of 1918), can be controlled with antibiotics.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Throat lavages and swabs provide suitable material. The latter must be placed in a suitable transport medium without delay to prevent them from drying out. Influenza viruses can be grown and isolated in cell cultures if the diagnostic specimen is obtained very early, i.e., in the first one or two days of the infection. Identification of the cultured viruses is achieved based on the hemagglutinating properties of the myxoviruses in the hemagglutination inhibition test or by means of immunofluorescence. If the specimen was obtained too late for virus isolation, a diagnosis can be arrived at by serological means, whereby a rise in the antibody titer of patient serum proves infection.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="classification-and-antigen-structure-of-influenza-a-viruses">Classification and Antigen Structure of Influenza A Viruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification-and-antigen-structure-of-influenza-a-viruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Viral prototype</th><th>Predominance</th><th>Antigen formula</th><th></th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td></td><td></td><td>Hemagglutinin (H)</td><td>Neuraminidase (N)</td></tr><tr><td>A/WS/33</td><td>1932–1946</td><td>HO</td><td>N1</td></tr><tr><td>A/PR8/34</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>A/Cambridge/46</td><td>1946–1957</td><td>H1</td><td>N1</td></tr><tr><td>A/F/M1/47</td><td></td><td></td><td></td></tr><tr><td>A/Singapore/57</td><td>1957–1968</td><td>H2</td><td>N2</td></tr><tr><td>A/Hong Kong/68</td><td>1968</td><td>H3</td><td>N2</td></tr><tr><td>A/USSR/77</td><td>1977</td><td>H1</td><td>N1</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="epidemiology">Epidemiology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Influenza A viruses are genetically variable. Slight antigenic changes are the general rule (antigenic drift, quasispecies) and are explained by selection of point mutants in the hemagglutinin under immunological pressure. More profound changes (antigenic shifts) explain the periodic occurrence of influenza A epidemics and pandemics.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="prevention-and-therapy">Prevention and therapy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention-and-therapy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>An inactivated adsorbate vaccine and some split vaccines (new: intranasal application) are available for influenza prophylaxis. The vaccine is recommended especially for persons whose occupation exposes them to such infections as well as persons with cardiovascular problems in their medical histories. The therapeutic options include amantadine, which inhibits the viral uncoating process, and more recently neuraminidase inhibitors. These substances shorten the duration of illness by blocking the release of the viruses from the host cells and their further dissemination in the body.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="bunyaviruses">Bunyaviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#bunyaviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The family Bunyaviridae comprises over 200 viral species, among them four human pathogen genera: Bunyavirus, Nairovirus, Phlebovirus, and Hantavirus. Bunyaviruses are spherical,  <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>80</mn><mo>−</mo><mn>110</mn><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">n</mi><mi mathvariant="normal">m</mi></mrow></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">80 - 110\mathrm{nm}</annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.7278em; vertical-align: -0.0833em;"></span><span class="mord">80</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span><span class="mbin">−</span><span class="mspace" style="margin-right: 0.2222em;"></span></span><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.6444em;"></span><span class="mord">110</span><span class="mord"><span class="mord mathrm">nm</span></span></span></span></span></span>  in size, and posses envelopes with spikes formed on membranes of the smooth endoplasmic reticulum. The genome consists of three antisense-strand RNA segments, whereby each segment produces a separate ribonucleoprotein complex, resulting in a unique feature of the virion: it contains three helical nucleocapsids.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10, May 2020) The document is available on</p>
<p>By Belay T.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture">Pathogenesis and Clinical Picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Genus Bunyavirus:</p>
<p>These viruses are transmitted by arthropods. They cause benign forms of encephalitis e.g. California Encephalitis Virus.</p>
<p>Genus Nairovirus:</p>
<p>The main human pathogen in this group is the Crimean-Congo hemorrhagic fever virus, with lethality rate as high as 50%. The virus is endemic to southeastern Europe, Central Asia, China, Saudi Arabia, and Africa and is transmitted by ticks as well as by direct contact with infected animals or patients.</p>
<p>Genus Phlebovirus:</p>
<p>This group includes the pathogens that cause the benign Pappataci or phlebotomus fever ("sandfly fever"), which occurs in Europe (Italy, Yugoslavia), North Africa, Asia, and South America and is transmitted by the phlebotomus sandfly. Rift Valley fever (RVF), an acute, febrile disease, rarely also involving hemorrhagic fever, is transmitted by mosquitoes and is endemic to Africa, usually following epizooties in livestock, in which case aerosol infection occurs (slaughtering). Epidemics have been reported with over 200,000 cases in Egypt and 25,000 cases in Senegal. Further epidemics have occurred in Somalia, Kenya, and Sudan.</p>
<p>Genus Hantavirus:</p>
<p>This genus includes several viral species (or serotypes) that can be classified into two groups according to the clinical symptoms they cause. The pathogens of nephropathica epidemica (NE), and hemorrhagic fever with renal syndrome (HFRS) and Hantavirus pulmonary syndrome (HPS).</p>
<p>The sources of infection are rodents (mice and rats). The infection is acquired by inhaling aerosols of urine, feces, and animal saliva. In NE and HFRS, a renal dysfunction follows the influenza like symptoms. HPS infection results in a rapidly progressive, acute dyspnea with pulmonary edema and is lethal in 60% of cases.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="serotypes-of-hantaviruses">Serotypes of Hantaviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#serotypes-of-hantaviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<table><thead><tr><th>Serotype</th><th>Syndrome</th><th>Geographic dissemination</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>Hantaan</td><td>HFRS, severe form</td><td>Asia, southeastern Europe</td></tr><tr><td>Belgrade</td><td>HFRS, severe form</td><td>Southeastern Europe</td></tr><tr><td>Puumala</td><td>NE</td><td>Central and northern Europe</td></tr><tr><td>Seoul</td><td>HFRS, mild form</td><td>Worldwide</td></tr><tr><td>Sin Nombre, etc.</td><td>HPS</td><td>US, Canada</td></tr></tbody></table>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>It is possible to isolate the virus from blood, but the procedure is too drawn-out and costly for routine diagnostics. Serology (IgM detection) is the method of choice, although the results can be difficult to interpret with bunyaviruses due to the rapidly changing antigenic variants produced in many of the viral species.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="epidemiology-and-prevention">Epidemiology and prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemiology-and-prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The bunyaviruses and phleboviruses are transmitted by bloodsucking arthropods, whereby the cycle involves either human and vector only or, as with the togaviruses and flaviviruses, a mammal- arthropod-mammal cycle actually independent of humans, and in which human victims represent a dead end for the infectious agent. Hantaviruses are transmitted aerogenically to humans from rodents, in which the viruses persist apathogenically for the lifespan of the animal.</p>
<p>Preventive measures include exposure prophylaxis (avoidance of insect bites and contact with rodents). An active vaccination is available for protection against Rift Valley fever.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="arenaviruses">Arenaviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#arenaviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Most members of the Arenaviridae family were first identified in the 1960s. The protoype arenavirus, the pathogen that causes lymphocytic choriomeningitis (LCM), was identified 30 years earlier, 1933. The human pathogens among the arenaviruses are the LCM virus (Europe, America), the Lassa virus (Africa), and the Junin and Machupo viruses (South America). All arenaviruses are spherical to pleomorphic. They consist of a "spiked" envelope derived from the plasma membrane, with an inner structure that appears to be granulated when viewed in ultrathin sections. It is to these granula the viral family owes its name (arenosus = sandy). They are considered to be host-cell ribosomes. The virion contains at least three strands of host RNA in addition to two viral RNA segments.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="ambisense-genome">Ambisense Genome<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#ambisense-genome" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The genome of the arenaviruses contains genomic components with sense (plus) polarity and others with antisense (minus) polarity (ambisense viruses, see p. 387) and is structured as follows: the smaller S part (S = small) codes in the 3′ part as an antisense-strand RNA for the nucleocapsid protein (NP) and in the 5′ part as sense-strand RNA for a viral glycoprotein. Each protein is translated separately from the subgenomic RNA; the NP, coded with the antisense orientation, is first transcribed into a sense-strand RNA. The L part (l = large) codes at the 3′ end in antisense-strand orientation for the viral polymerase and at the 5′ end in sense-strand orientation for a regulatory RNA-binding protein.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 11), May 2020
This document is available on
by Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture">Pathogenesis and clinical picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The source of nearly all human arenavirus infections is to be found in rodents. The virus enters the body per os, aerogenically or possibly also by skin contact. A pronounced viremia develops at first, followed by organ manifestations. In the case of LCM, these are normally harmless and flulike, although they can also develop into meningitis or encephalitis, in rare cases with a lethal outcome.</p>
<p>The Lassa virus is pantropic. It causes a hemorrhagic fever affecting nearly all inner organs and has a high rate of lethality. Death results from shock and anoxia</p>
<p>The clinical picture resulting from Junin and Machupo virus infections is similar to lassa virus. Compared to Lassa infections, CNS involvement is more frequent and the lethality rate is somewhat lower with these two viruses.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>In the acute stage, arenaviruses can be isolated from the patient's blood. Postmortem isolation is best done from liver tissue. In the hemorrhagic fevers, especially Lassa fever, the blood is highly infectious and handling it requires proper precautions and utmost care (aerosol formation!). Isolation of the virus is relatively easy in cell cultures. For reasons of safety, only special high-security laboratories are qualified to handle these organisms (e.g., at the Centers for Disease Control and Prevention in Atlanta, GA, USA). Serodiagnosis is also feasible using standard serological techniques.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="epidemiology-and-prevention">Epidemiology and prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemiology-and-prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>All arenaviruses are endemic to rodents and are transmitted to humans by these animals. No specific immunoprophylactic tools have been developed for any of these viruses. As far as exposure prophylaxis is concerned, it must be remembered that the LCM, Junin, and Machupo viruses are not transmitted among humans, but the Lassa virus is transmitted by this route. The most stringent precautions are therefore called for when treating Lassa patients. Healthcare staff must wear special clothing and facemasks and special reduced-pressure plastic tents are recommended as patient cubicles. The therapeutic tools available for treatment are ribavirin and human immunoglobulin.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="paramyxoviruses">Paramyxoviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#paramyxoviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This family includes the genera:</p>
<ul>
<li>Parmyxovirus with the parainfluenza viruses</li>
<li>Rubulavirus with the mumps virus</li>
<li>Morbillivirus with the measles virus</li>
<li>Pneumovirus with the respiratory syncytial virus (RS)</li>
<li>Nonclassified paramyxoviruses (Hendra, Nipah)</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="family-paramyxoviridae">Family Paramyxoviridae<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#family-paramyxoviridae" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The family Paramyxoviridae is a heterogeneous, both in its biology and pathogenic properties. It is divided into two subfamilies:</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020</p>
<p>By Belay T.</p>
<p>Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Paramyxovirinae</li>
<li>Pneumovirinae</li>
</ul>
<p>Paramyxovirinae with the genera Paramyxovirus with the human pathogen species parainfluenza virus types 1 and 3. Rubulavirus with the mumps virus and parainfluenza virus types 2 and 4. Morbillivirus with the human pathogen measles virus and several zoopathic species that cause severe respiratory infections in various animal species (dogs cats, cattle, seals, dolphins, turtles). The nonclassified, closely related zoopathic and human pathogen Hendra and Nipah viruses. Pneumovirinae, genus Pneumovirus, probably with several types of RS viruses (respiratory syncytial virus).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="structure-of-the-paramyxoviruses">Structure of the Paramyxoviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#structure-of-the-paramyxoviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>All paramyxoviruses have a similar structure, they are pleomorphic. The smallest forms are 120–150 nm in size (with the exception of the somewhat smaller RS virus). They also possess an envelope that encloses the nucleocapsid. The envelope is derived from the cell membrane. The genome consists of a continuous, single-stranded antisense RNA. Various viral proteins are integrated in the envelope, visible in the form of spikes.</p>
<p>The generic taxons are based on these spikes:</p>
<ul>
<li>parainfluenza and mumps viruses have two types of spikes, one containing the hemagglutinin (i.e., possessing hemagglutination activity) coupled with neuraminidase (HN protein), and the other the so-called fusion (F) protein, responsible for fusion of the envelope with the cell membrane.</li>
<li>Measles viruses contain no neuraminidase</li>
<li>Pneumoviruses possess only the F protein</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture">Pathogenesis and clinical picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The parainfluenza viruses cause flulike infections, mainly in small children, which occasionally progress to bronchitis or even pneumonia. Occasionally, a dangerous croup syndrome develops. Bacterial superinfections are frequent, as are the usually harmless reinfections.</p>
<p>In mumps virus infections, the virus first replicates in the respiratory tract, then causes a viremia, after which a parotitis is the main development as well as, fairly frequently, mumps meningitis. Complications include infection of various glandular organs. Orchitis can occur in post puberty boys who contract mumps.</p>
<p>The pathogenesis of measles has not been fully explained. It is assumed that the virus, following primary replication in lymphoid tissues, is distributed hematogenously. Thereafter the oral mucosa displays an enanthem and the tiny white “Koplik’s spots”. Then the fever once again rises and the typical measles exanthem manifests. Possible complications include otitis in the form of a bacterial superinfection as well as pneumonia and encephalitis. A rare late sequel of measles (one case per million inhabitants) is subacute sclerosing panencephalitis (SSPE) in which nucleocapsids accumulate in brain cells, whereby few or</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10) May 2020
The document is available on
BY
Studocu
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>no viral progeny are produced for lack of matrix protein. This disease occurs between the ages of one and 20, involves loss of memory and personality changes, and usually results in death within six to 12 months.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="measles-exanthem">Measles Exanthem<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#measles-exanthem" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20251219-1ec40b2e.jpeg" alt="img-50.jpeg">
The typical exanthem manifests during what is presumably the Second hematogenous disseminative episode of the morbilliviruses</p>
<p>Nipah and Hendra virus infections are zoonoses endemic to Southeast Asia (Nipah) or Australia (Hendra). Both infections result in encephalitis with relatively high lethality rates (up to 40%) and in some cases severe interstitial pneumonias.</p>
<p>RS viruses cause bronchiolitis or pneumonia, mainly in children up to six months of age, or rarely up to two years. Immune status appears to play an important role in the course of the infection. It has been determined that the course of the disease is more severe in children who have received dead vaccine material (similarly to measles). This is presumably due to antibodies, in the case of small children the mother's antibodies acquired by diaplacental transport. Immunosuppressed patients, for instance, bone marrow recipients, are also at risk for RSV.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Serodiagnostic methods</li>
<li>direct detection tests based on immunofluorescence or enzyme immunoassay</li>
<li>Paramyxoviruses replicate readily in cell cultures from human tissues</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="epidemiology">Epidemiology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Paramyxoviruses are transmitted by droplet infection. Generalized contamination levels in the population (except for Nipah and Hendra) are already very high in childhood (90% in 10-year-old children for parainfluenza virus types 1–3). Nipah and Hendra viruses are zoonoses</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020</p>
<p>By Belay T.</p>
<p>Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>that are transmitted to humans from animals (Nipah: pigs, Hendra: horses). Various different animals can be infected by these pathogens, but bats (Pteropus) appear to be the natural reservoir for both viruses.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="prevention">Prevention<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#prevention" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Attenuated live vaccines are available for measles and mumps. The dead vaccine should not be used due to the aggravating effect mentioned above. No vaccines have as yet been developed for the other parainfluenza viruses.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="rhabdoviruses">Rhabdoviruses<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rhabdoviruses" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The rhabdoviruses of significance in human medicine are classified in seven genotypes. Type 1 is the classic, worldwide type that occurs in two forms: the "street virus" isolated from humans and animals and the "virus fixe" according to Pasteur. In 1882, Pasteur had transmitted the virus intracerebrally to rabbits. Following repeated passages of the virus in the rabbits, he had developed a dead vaccine type. Due to the brain-to-brain passages in the laboratory animals, the "virus fixe" became so highly adapted to brain tissue that it was unable to replicate in extraneural tissues. Types 2-4 were isolated from African bats, types 5 and 6 from European bats, and type 7 from Australian bats.</p>
<p>Rhabdoviruses are rodlike with one end flat and one end rounded ("bulletshaped") and a spiked envelope surrounding a nucleocapsid similar to that of the myxoviruses. The genome consists of antisense strand RNA</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture">Pathogenesis and clinical picture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pathogenesis-and-clinical-picture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Rabies viruses are almost always transmitted by the bite, sometimes also the scratch, of a rabid animal. The virus at first replicates at the portal of entry in muscle and connective tissue, then wanders along the nerve cells into the CNS, where more viral replication takes place. Using the same route, the virus then disseminates from the CNS into peripheral organs, above all the salivary glands, cornea, and kidneys. The primary clinical picture is anencephalitis with lethal outcome for humans and animals.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10) May 2020
The document is available on
by Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="clinical-course-of-rabies">Clinical Course of Rabies<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#clinical-course-of-rabies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The disease goes through three stages. The initial, or prodromal, stage involves itching and burning at the portal of entry (bite wound), nausea, vomiting, and possibly a melancholy mood. In the second or excitative stage, cramps and spasms of the pharynx and larynx are the main symptoms, rendering swallowing very painful. The spasms can be induced by the mere sight of water ("hydrophobia"). Other mild acoustic and visual stimuli may elicit exaggerated reactions including attacks of cramps and violent anger, hitting, biting, and screaming. Death occurs within three to four days at the earliest. The third, paralytic, stage may develop instead of early death, with ascending paralysis and asphyxia, leading to exitus. Therapy is exclusively symptomatic. Since the patient experiences the disease in a fully conscious state, most of the medication serves to alleviate the pain and anxiety states. The disease runs essentially the same course in humans and animals, whereby the behavior of animals is often radically altered: wild animals lose their fear of humans and tame pets become aggressive. Rabies with the excitative stage is known as "furious rabies," without it as "dumb rabies."</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="diagnosis">Diagnosis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#diagnosis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is usually made on the basis of clinical signs. There are no tests to confirm rabies with absolute certainty while the patient is still alive. Viruses can be detected by laboratory investigation of the patient's brain after death. The test is not usually carried out in countries with few resources.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="epidemiology">Epidemiology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#epidemiology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Lyssavirus type 1 is endemic to North America and Europe in wild animals (sylvatic rabies) and in certain tropical areas in domestic pets as well, in particular dogs (urban rabies). The reservoir for the remaining lyssavirus types are bloodsucking (hemovorous) as well as fructivorous and insectivorous bats. The virus is excreted with the saliva of the diseased or terminal incubator animal and enters other animals or humans through scratch or bite wounds. The virus is highly labile, so transmission on contaminated objects is very rare. Human-to-human transmission has not been confirmed with the exception of cases in which rabies in corneal donors had gone unnoticed.</p>
<p>What you should do if someone is bitten by a dog?</p>
<ul>
<li>First aid</li>
<li>Post-exposure prophylaxis</li>
</ul>
<p>Why first aid and PEP?</p>
<p>Wound care and anti-rabies treatment after a dog bite can reduce the occurrence of rabies in a bitten person by up to 90%.</p>
<p>First aid</p>
<p>Immediately after a dog bite, you should thoroughly clean and flush the wound with soap and water, detergent, or a substance that kills viruses, such as 70% alcohol, tincture of aqueous solution of iodine, or povidone iodine. Continue flushing the wound for at least 15</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020
By Belay T.
Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>minutes. The wound should not be sutured (stitched) unless this is essential to stop heavy bleeding. If stitches are required, the wound should not be sutured until after post-exposure prophylaxis has occurred.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="post-exposure-prophylaxis">Post-exposure prophylaxis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#post-exposure-prophylaxis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>If a person is bitten by a dog in countries where rabies is endemic, there is no way of being certain that the animal is free from rabies. The bitten person should be given post-exposure prophylaxis as soon as possible after the bite. Every year, around 15 million people receive this treatment worldwide, preventing an estimated 327,000 human deaths from rabies</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="who-pep-guidelines">WHO PEP Guidelines<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#who-pep-guidelines" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>WHO has published the guidelines for PEP following different levels of contact with a suspected/confirmed rabid animal (WHO. Available at <a href="http://www.who.ch/programmes/emc/vph.htm" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">http://www.who.ch/programmes/emc/vph.htm</a>)</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>Category</th><th>Type of Contact with a Suspect or Confirmed Rabid Domestic or Wild Animal, or Animal Unavailable for Observation</th><th>Recommended Rx</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>I</td><td>Touching or feeding of animals; licks on intact skin</td><td>None, if reliable case history is available</td></tr><tr><td>II</td><td>Nibbling of uncovered skin; minor scratches or abrasions without bleeding; licks on broken skin</td><td>Administer vaccine immediately; stop treatment if animal remains healthy throughout an observation Period of 10 days or if animal is euthanized and found to be negative for rabies by appropriate lab. techniques</td></tr><tr><td>III</td><td>Single or multiple transdermal bites or scratches; contamination of mucous membrane with saliva (i.e licks)</td><td>Administer rabies Ig and vaccine immediately; stop treatment if animal remains healthy throughout an observation period of 10 days or if animal is killed humanely and found to be negative for rabies by appropriate lab. techniques</td></tr></tbody></table>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="pre-exposure-prophylaxis">Pre-exposure Prophylaxis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#pre-exposure-prophylaxis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>It is indicated for those with a relatively high risk of rabies exposure; includes veterinarians, workers in laboratories using the virus, spelunkers, and those planning to visit countries with a high prevalence of dog rabies for more than 30 days.</p>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (vol 10) May 2020. The document is available on</p>
<p>By Belay T.</p>
<p>Downloaded by dspop Service Delivery (<a href="mailto:dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">dspopservicedelivery@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="rabies-vaccines">Rabies vaccines<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rabies-vaccines" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Two types of vaccines exist</li>
<li>Older type</li>
<li>Made from nerve tissue infected with rabies virus</li>
<li>Given intramuscularly (IM)</li>
<li>Newer type</li>
<li>Made from virus-infected cells grown (cultured) in the laboratory</li>
<li>Safer and more effective</li>
<li>WHO recommends that the rabies cell-culture vaccine (rabies CCV) should be used in preference to the nerve tissue vaccine wherever possible</li>
<li>Rabies CCV can be given either by intramuscular injection, or intradermally (ID) into the upper arm</li>
<li>Intradermal route has been shown to be as safe and effective as the traditional intramuscular route, and is cheaper because it requires less vaccine</li>
<li>Modern CCVs should be given in four or five doses</li>
<li>Dosage for each injection depends on the vaccine type and the route of administration</li>
<li>Intramuscular dosages are 0.5 ml or 1.0 ml</li>
<li>Intradermal dosage is only 0.1 ml</li>
<li>For rabies PEP, you should tell the patient and their family that it is essential to return at fixed intervals for repeat vaccinations in order to prevent rabies</li>
<li>The intramuscular regimen is given at days 0, 3, 7, 14 and 28 after the exposure</li>
<li>The intradermal regimen is given at days 0, 3, 7, 28 and 90 after exposure</li>
<li>There is also a rabies vaccine which is administered at days 0, 7 and 28 days (IM), and rabies duck embryo vaccine, which is administered at day 0, 7 and 21 days (IM or ID)</li>
<li>For both these vaccines, a booster dose is given after two to three years</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="rabies-ig">Rabies Ig<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#rabies-ig" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<ul>
<li>Specific protection in humans with Category III exposure is provided by injecting a human or equine (horse) immunoglobulin at the site of the bite, as soon as possible after exposure to neutralize the virus</li>
<li>The term ‘immunoglobulin’ refers to a preparation of antibodies made either in humans or in horses who have been vaccinated against rabies</li>
<li>Antibodies from their blood which attack the rabies viruses are harvested and stabilized in an injectable liquid</li>
<li>As much as possible of the dose of rabies immunoglobulin is given into, or as near as possible to, the site of the bite</li>
<li>Human rabies Ig is given in a single dose of 20 international units (IU) per kilogram (kg) of the person’s body weight</li>
<li>Horse rabies Ig is cheaper, but less effective and more likely to produce adverse allergic reactions; it is given in a single dose of 40 IU/kg</li>
</ul>
<p>Medical Virology Lecture Note for MLS students (year-III), May 2020
By Belay T.
